A year ago, ChatGPT felt untouchable. Not anymore.

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·31:21 ·4.0K views · 2026-02-05
Sam Altman OpenAI Anthropic Keynote
> "It's a broad sweeping mass exodus of software stocks.
TL;DR: The AI landscape is experiencing unprecedented acceleration, with a fierce rivalry between OpenAI and Anthropic driving rapid advancements in AI agents and development tools that are making software creation effectively "free" and reshaping the entire industry from...

TL;DR: The AI landscape is experiencing unprecedented acceleration, with a fierce rivalry between OpenAI and Anthropic driving rapid advancements in AI agents and development tools that are making software creation effectively "free" and reshaping the entire industry from enterprise to individual users.

The Intensifying AI Arms Race

  • Accelerated Competition: The market is now a "full-on war for the enterprise, for the developer, and for the consumer," marked by rapid product releases and direct rivalry.
      • Anthropic's Opus 4.6: Released as their most powerful model, capable of working directly within applications like PowerPoint, compiling regulatory filings, and orchestrating teams of agents in parallel.
      • OpenAI's Frontier: Countered with a new enterprise platform designed for building and managing AI agents that can perform real work across entire businesses.
  • Public Rivalry: Anthropic ran a Super Bowl ad subtly mocking OpenAI, prompting a strong response from OpenAI CEO Sam Altman, who called Anthropic "authoritarian" amid debates over "elitism in AI."
  • Viral Agent Adoption: OpenClaw (later Moldbot), an open-source AI agent built by a single developer, has gone viral for its ability to hook into computers and autonomously perform tasks like booking or building.
      • While OpenAI's Codex is a powerful coding model and an internal favorite, it is "still struggling to get the same kind of buzz" as Claude Code and projects like OpenClaw.

The New Paradigm of Software Development

  • Software Becoming "Free": Guillermo Rauch, co-founder and CEO of Vercel, notes that "software is now effectively free" as models automate and generate traditional software, leading to a "mass exodus of software stocks" and spreading sell-offs from CRMs to legal tech and video games.
  • Explosive "Vibe Coding" Growth: Vercel, which hosts many new applications, experienced 14 times larger growth in new deployments from November to December than the previous year, with people "vibe coding" (informal, rapid development using AI) even during holidays.
      • This "vibe coding" is quickly evolving "from prototype to production grade applications," leveraging the same global cloud infrastructure, security, and frameworks used by the largest internet properties.
  • Shift to "Just In Time" Software: Traditional, expensive "static and inert software" with month-long procurement cycles is being replaced by prompt-generated, purpose-built applications that can be deployed "with basically two prompts."
      • This will lead to "downward pricing pressure" and a rise in "personal software" and "throwaway software" tailored for immediate needs.

Agentic AI: Capabilities, Security, and Cloud Infrastructure

  • Superpowers of Agentic Models: Anthropic's models excel at "agentic loops" and "tool calling," enabling agents to "use tools just like us human engineers do" to read errors, iterate, and automate processes.
      • This signifies a move towards "automating the operating system," where agents operate computers on behalf of users (engineers, designers, marketers) to deliver desired outputs.
  • User-Friendly Computing: OpenClaw (Moldbot) exemplifies this by allowing users to interact with a "super intelligent engineer" via text message to automate complex tasks like unsubscribing from spam newsletters. This represents the "most user-friendly computing device in history."
  • Security and Control: While agents gain power from access to tools (email, calendar), secure deployment is crucial. Vercel provides "sandboxes" for agents, incorporating firewalls and virtualization to manage risks, snapshot, and restore if issues arise.
  • Bullish on Cloud Infrastructure: The future will involve "millions or trillions of these digital workers," making cloud infrastructure vital for hosting and scaling these AI applications and agents.

The Evolving Competitive Landscape and Model Choices

  • Specialized Strengths: OpenAI and Anthropic each have distinct strengths: Anthropic excels in computer tool-calling and agentic tasks, while OpenAI's Codex is powerful for "expert software engineering tasks."
      • Developers are increasingly using a "combination of models and tools" to leverage these strengths, making "the developers and end users" the ultimate winners.
  • "Modelism is the Future": Users and developers will dynamically route tasks to a "huge portfolio of models" based on complexity and cost.
      • Frontier Models: For complex "knowledge work," "frontier science problems," or "really hard research tasks," the "absolute most intelligent model" (e.g., Opus 4.6, GPT-5 Pro) is essential. Vercel's support agent, for instance, automates 87% of difficult tickets using the most intelligent models.
      • Cost-Effective Alternatives: For simpler tasks or when facing rate limits and price performance issues with major labs, open-source and Chinese models (like Moonshots Kimmy or fine-tuned Quen) offer cheaper, efficient, and "almost as good" alternatives, even for consumer applications.
  • Caution on Benchmarks: Guillermo Rauch advises "caution when looking at benchmarks," as many are "gamified" and "don't tell the full story." Real success is measured by "stickiness, retention, and NPS" (Net Promoter Score).
  • Discontinuity and Empowerment: AI is experiencing "yet another moment where there's been a discontinuity in the growth of capabilities." Things AI "cannot do" are proven wrong "two weeks later."
      • Individuals are immensely empowered, with "one person billion dollar company well within sight" and the ability to "provision their own agents" and "scale myself."

Notable Quotes

"It's a broad sweeping mass exodus of software stocks. And I might put it another way. It has been mostly a bloodbath." — Jared Draosa "We saw 14 times larger growth from November to December than we saw last year. So what's happening is software is now effectively free." — Guillermo Rauch "Vibe coding is sort of going through its teenage years into adulthood very very quickly and now truly everybody can create software." — Guillermo Rauch "Every single thing that we've said AI cannot do gets proven wrong two weeks later. And so I I I always tell people, get really good at using these AI tools. Your leverage will be immense." — Guillermo Rauch

We're just constantly all day, every day, how can we make this CNBC segment, how can we test it? How can we actually build something to show our audience? And it surprises us day after day. Guillermo, this was a wonderful conversation. Come back and talk to us again. There's no shortage of news ever, and the model, the iterations, everything just keeps coming so quickly. So, we hope you'll come back and thank you for taking the time. See you next time. Arm CEO Says Data Center Business Is 'Exploding'

Bloomberg Technology(715.0K) ·10:18 ·539 views · 2026-02-05
Jensen Huang NVIDIA OpenAI Earnings
Arm CEO Rene Haas reports record revenue and royalties, driven by an "exploding" data center business (100%+ YoY growth) that is on track to surpass handsets as Arm's largest segment sooner than anticipated due to pervasive global AI investments.
TL;DR Arm CEO Rene Haas reports record revenue and royalties, driven by an "exploding" data center business (100%+ YoY growth) that is on track to surpass handsets as Arm's largest segment sooner than anticipated due to pervasive global AI investments. Exceptional Financial...

TL;DR

Arm CEO Rene Haas reports record revenue and royalties, driven by an "exploding" data center business (100%+ YoY growth) that is on track to surpass handsets as Arm's largest segment sooner than anticipated due to pervasive global AI investments.

Record Performance and Shifting Market Focus

  • Exceptional Financial Results: Arm achieved record revenue of $1.24 billion and record royalties of nearly $740 million, marking a 27% year-on-year increase.
  • Data Center Business Explosion: The data center segment grew over 100% year-on-year, significantly contributing to Arm's performance and is projected to become the company's largest business within a few years.
  • Minimal Handset Market Impact:
      • Despite broader industry challenges like memory shortages affecting the smartphone market, Arm's diversified business across multiple sectors (including the rapidly growing data center) minimized direct impact.
      • Arm's products are primarily used in higher-end devices, which are less affected by shortages that typically impact lower-royalty-rate products at the bottom end of the stack.
      • The company raised its guidance for the next quarter.

Hyperscaler Dominance and AI Integration

  • Accelerated Data Center Inflection: Arm anticipates its data center business will become its largest segment "sooner than we had thought," largely due to rapidly increasing demand for Arm CPUs.
  • Dominant Hyperscaler Market Share: Arm now holds over 50% market share among hyperscalers.
  • Increasing CPU Core Count:
      • Next-generation chips are integrating significantly more Arm cores: e.g., Graviton processors moving from 96 to 192 cores, and NVIDIA's Vera (previously Grace) growing from 72 to 88 cores.
      • More cores directly translate to higher royalties for Arm.
  • CPU's Critical Role in AI: The rapid growth in core count is driven by "agentic AI" workloads (moving, managing, and orchestrating workflows) which CPUs are uniquely suited to handle.

Sustained AI Investment and Market Opportunity

  • Unprecedented Capital Expenditure: Major tech companies like Google (Alphabet), Meta, and Microsoft are announcing investments in data center infrastructure that significantly exceed previous expectations, indicating a long-term commitment to AI.
      • Examples include Google's potential $185 billion CapEx and the "Stargate initiative" ($500 billion over several years), which previously seemed improbable but is now viewed as credible.
  • AI as the "Final Frontier": Rene Haas views AI as a transformative technology with a "long, long runway" for growth, having barely penetrated large enterprises, health systems, or drug discovery.
  • Justifying Massive Investments: Despite market nervousness due to the unprecedented scale of investment, the potential for AI to transform "how everything is done" necessitates continued heavy investment.

Strategic Partnerships and Arm's Ubiquitous Role

  • NVIDIA's Standalone CPUs: Arm is "thrilled" by NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang's decision to sell Vera CPUs as standalone products.
      • This initiative supports the trend toward a homogeneous Arm-based stack in data centers, simplifying maintenance, upgrades, and reducing costs while offering flexibility.
      • The Vera Rubin platform, for example, uses six times the number of CPUs compared to Grace Blackwell for storage, DPU, and offload functions.
  • Pervasive AI and Custom Chips: Arm works with a vast array of industry players, including NVIDIA, OpenAI (reportedly with Broadcom for custom chips), and Meta (also exploring custom chips).
  • AI Workloads "Everywhere": Investment is not confined to data centers; the industry is focused on running AI workloads across all devices – wearables, smartphones, PCs, and physical AI applications like autonomous driving and robotics.
  • Arm's Foundational Advantage: In a rapidly evolving AI landscape where predicting future architectures is challenging, Arm's programmable, flexible, and low-power IP positions it strongly to drive AI workloads regardless of where chips land.
  • SoftBank Stake Commitment: Arm's largest shareholder, Masayoshi Son, remains "very long on ARM" with no intention to sell, demonstrating strong confidence in the company's future growth, which has exceeded initial IPO projections.

Notable Quotes

The memory situation isn't really impacting ARM so directly." — Rene Haas, Arm CEO "It's very clear, and that's why in the earnings call, I I said in a few years. As you know, we don't typically provide forward guidance on an annual basis, but we have a line of sight that says we see it coming. And it's probably coming sooner than we had thought." — Rene Haas, Arm CEO "The AI opportunity, the way I like to think about it is kind of the final frontier relative to technology. When you think about what could be on be be beyond AI, it's really hard to imagine." — Rene Haas, Arm CEO "What that means is inside the data center, you're gonna start to see movement towards a homogeneous type of structure where people would love to have the ARM stack running almost everything. It's just easier from a maintenance standpoint. It's easier from an upgrade standpoint. It's easier from a cost standpoint." — Rene Haas, Arm CEO

If you think about what we've done since we went public in 2023, we told the world we'd be growing at 25% year-on-year for two, three years, and people didn't believe it was possible. CEO Fireside Chat With Jensen Huang and Pascal Daloz at Dassault Systèmes 3DEXPERIENCE World

NVIDIA(2.1M) ·37:04 ·38.4K views · 2026-02-05
Jensen Huang Howard Marks NVIDIA Amazon Keynote
NVIDIA and Dassault Systèmes announced a significant long-term strategic partnership, integrating Dassault Systèmes’ Virtual Twin technologies with NVIDIA’s AI and Omniverse platforms to create science-validated "World Models" that will redefine indu...
TL;DR NVIDIA and Dassault Systèmes announced a significant long-term strategic partnership, integrating Dassault Systèmes’ Virtual Twin technologies with NVIDIA’s AI and Omniverse platforms to create science-validated "World Models" that will redefine industrial design,...

TL;DR

NVIDIA and Dassault Systèmes announced a significant long-term strategic partnership, integrating Dassault Systèmes’ Virtual Twin technologies with NVIDIA’s AI and Omniverse platforms to create science-validated "World Models" that will redefine industrial design, manufacturing, and operations through generative AI and real-time simulation.

The Evolving Partnership & Generative AI

  • A Quarter-Century Collaboration: NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang and Dassault Systèmes CEO Pascal Daloz highlighted their long-standing partnership, which began over 25 years ago with OpenGL (precursor to today's RTX) and CGFX (precursor to CUDA), revolutionizing workstation computing.
  • New Computing Platform: The current collaboration marks a "reinvention of the computing platform," scaling Dassault Systèmes' 40-year vision of virtual twins to represent the physical world at a much larger scale through AI.
  • Generative Economy: Industry is shifting from structured design representations (specifying every geometry) to "generative computing models" where AI is foundational.
  • AI as Infrastructure: Jensen Huang emphasized that AI is becoming a fundamental infrastructure, akin to water, electricity, or the internet, powering every industry and country globally.
  • 100% Digital Future: The future of design, simulation, validation, and software integration is projected to be entirely digital, with Dassault Systèmes' VAs (CATIA, SOLIDWORKS, SIMULIA, BIOVIA, DELMIA, ENOVIA) leveraging NVIDIA's technologies.
  • Massive Integration: This is the largest collaboration between the two companies, involving the fusion of NVIDIA CUDA-X acceleration libraries, NVIDIA AI (for physical and agentic AI), and NVIDIA Omniverse (NVIDIA's digital twin platform) into Dassault Systèmes’ offerings.
  • Unprecedented Scale: This integration promises to accelerate computing by 100x, 1000x, and soon up to 1,000,000x, enabling real-time operations for tasks like wind tunnel simulations and robot orchestration.

Revolutionizing Design & Engineering with World Models

  • Inventing the Future: The partnership empowers Dassault Systèmes' community of 45 million users, 400,000 customers, and 15+ million engineers to "invent the future," moving beyond mere automation of the past.
  • The World Model Foundation: A core concept is the "World Model," grounded in biology, physics, and material sciences, which is crucial for computing complex systems, including life itself.
      • Computing Life's Language: This involves learning the language of DNA, proteins, and cells to understand interactions and properties.
      • Generative Biology & Materials: The ability to translate between human and biological languages, generating new proteins for drugs, chemicals, or materials with enhanced properties (e.g., stronger, lighter, more heat-resistant).
      • Case Study (Bell Group): The Bell Group uses virtual twins powered by a biological world model to automatically generate proteins for healthier, more sustainable non-dairy foods, leading to faster innovation and certified decisions.
  • Infinite Possibilities & Physics AI: The virtual twin can explore an "infinite of possibilities" by fusing simulation with AI, using "surrogate and emulation models."
      • NVIDIA Physics Nemo: This physics-aware AI model simulation system works alongside or is trained by principled simulators, predicting physical behavior 10,000 times faster while remaining grounded in the laws of physics.
      • Case Study (Lucid Motors): Lucid Motors embeds crash behavior, aerodynamics, and vehicle performance early in development, allowing engineers to design vehicle *behavior* (not just shape) and certify it.

Software-Defined Factories & Industrialization

  • Virtual Twin Factories: Factories are evolving into hybrid physical-virtual assets, designed, simulated, and operated entirely within virtual twins. This allows for complex simulations of millions of objects, optimizing layouts, sequences, and robot operations.
      • Case Study (Omron): Omron engineers "software-defined factories" where autonomous elements are integrated from day one, resulting in more flexible, resilient, and adaptive production systems.
  • The AI Factory Revolution: AI is driving a new industrial revolution, requiring the rapid scaling of three interconnected industries:
      • Chip Factories: Increasing production of semiconductor and packaging facilities.
      • Computer Factories: Manufacturing supercomputers from these chips.
      • AI Factories: Housing supercomputers to "manufacture" AIs themselves.
  • Largest Industrial Buildout: A gigawatt AI factory can cost approximately $50 billion, contributing to tens of gigawatts being built worldwide – representing the largest industrial infrastructure buildout in human history, potentially worth trillions over the next decade.
  • MBSE for AI Factories: NVIDIA uses Dassault Systèmes' Model-Based Systems Engineering (MBSE) to design, plan, and simulate AI factories (including data centers, supercomputers, networks, energy, cooling) within a virtual twin *before* construction, saving significant time and money.
  • "Looks Real, Is Real": The virtual twins offer precise digital representations, integrating industrial bills of material (BOMs) and allowing for advance validation, preventing costly mistakes in complex builds like a $50 billion AI factory.

The Augmented Engineer & Knowledge Protection

  • Virtual Companions: AI agents will serve as "companions" to engineers, augmenting the design process rather than replacing them. Every designer will have a team of AI companions trained to perform specific tasks.
      • Structured & Unstructured Data: AI facilitates the conversion of unstructured information (photos, videos) into precise, controllable 3D structured data for enhanced design.
      • Revolutionized Workflow: Workflows will be transformed, enabling rapid progression from a 2D sketch to a parametric 3D model with automatic simulation in seconds.
  • Engineers as Architects: Engineers will manage and direct their AI companions, who can explore design options, optimize parameters, and present multiple solutions for human fine-tuning. This will significantly increase the number of users of Dassault Systèmes' tools, encompassing both human and AI agents.
  • Compliance by Design:
      • Case Study (NAR): The National Institute for Aviation Research (NAR) can use virtual companions to automatically ingest and constantly verify compliance with the 10,000+ requirements for airplane certification, shifting compliance upstream to become a competitive edge.
  • From Language Model to World Model:
      • Language Models: Understand syntax, vocabulary, and "taste" in language.
      • World Models: Go further by obeying the laws of physics, understanding causality (inertia, friction, gravity), and learning through simulation and examples.
      • "Shift Left" for Design: This allows for manufacturability, regulatory compliance (integrating human values), and component approval to be integrated upstream into the design process, ensuring "good to go" designs.
  • Protecting Knowledge: AI companions will codify individual designers' skills, preferences, habits, and domain expertise, creating a personalized, private repository of knowledge that enhances individual productivity without being public or open-sourced.

Notable Quotes

"Everything that we do is in the digital world. 40 years ago, Dassault Systèmes revolutionized the idea of virtual twins... now we're going to represent the physical world at a much, much larger scale in a completely revolutionized computer, an AI computer." — Jensen Huang, NVIDIA CEO "They don't want to automate the past. They want to invent the future." — Pascal Daloz, Dassault Systèmes CEO "AI is foundational to every single industry, it is going to become an infrastructure. Just as water was infrastructure, electricity is infrastructure, internet was infrastructure, now artificial intelligence will be infrastructure." — Jensen Huang, NVIDIA CEO "It is very likely and I'm certain this is going to happen that every designer... will have a team of companions and you've trained these companions... they're all going to be using Dassault Systèmes tools." — Jensen Huang, NVIDIA CEO

Actually, the virtual twin is exploring an infinite of possibilities. So the question I have for you, could we compute infinity? Eight years ago, I introduced the idea to scientific computing and simulations: the idea that in the future, not only will we use principled simulations where the equations, the laws of physics, are well understood and well represented, however, the simulation time takes way too long. Why don't we augment that with generative methods of predicting the future using artificial intelligence? It's a little bit like the analogy I would give. It's a little bit like dogs able to catch a ball out of the air, and yet they're not doing physics simulations of balls bouncing or the elastic nature of the ball. They're just literally watching us and predicting where it's going to go, and they snatch it out of the air. So the idea that an AI could learn how to predict physics and learn how to predict very accurately how materials would crumble, what happens to a crash—those capabilities are within grasp. We have a technology called Physics NeMo. Physics NeMo is essentially a physics-aware AI model simulation system and AI framework that allows us to create these AI models that are either trained by principled simulators or work alongside principled simulators. So it's grounded in the laws of physics but able to predict 10,000 times faster. Now if everything is already running in real-time, then you can predict it 10,000 times greater scale. That's just where we are right now. Imagine where we're going to be in the future. The idea of simulation and emulation coming together to help you design is going to be really revolutionary. Again, this is exactly what you see here with a customer called Lucid. We know them; they are one of the most innovative car companies in the world. What do they do? They embed the crash behavior, the aerodynamics, the vehicle performance upstream, early in the vehicle program developments. So the engineers don't only design the shape; they design the behavior, and we certify it. This is exactly what you say. This is this vision empowering designers and engineers and also unlocking business people to develop delightful experiences for their customers. Now let's talk about factories. Operating a factory of these gigantic scales inside a virtual twin is extraordinarily complex. A factory is not just one object; it's millions of objects. We want to simulate or emulate how these factories will operate in the real world so that we could arrange the manufacturing lines properly, arrange it in the right sequence, space it properly, organize the robots within it, run the robot AIs so that these AI robots could be operating inside the factory, manipulating things, assembling things, moving things, keeping things safe. All of this is going to happen inside a virtual twin. The products that Dassault is going to help people build and design are going to become gigantic in the future. Again, this is exactly what we do with Omron. You've seen on the screen, they don't use a virtual twin only to visualize the factories; in fact, they do much more. They engineer what we call a software-defined factory. That's right. The difference is coming from the fact that they are designing the autonomous part day one. It's not something they infuse when the production system is already up and running. As a result, those factories become much more flexible, resilient, and adaptive. That's why it's an industrial revolution. Just as energy did that, just as mechanical energy, power did that, just as electricity, and of course, the internet did all that, we're now seeing artificial intelligence doing that. The language model has to understand syntax, vocabulary, and structure of language, and it has taste. What's a better way to compose a paragraph? And it has guardrails—what are the things it should talk about and things it should avoid talking about? In a world model, instead of taste and values, it has to obey the laws of physics. It has to understand causality: if you tip over a domino, all of the dominoes that are connected to it or nearby it will be tipped over. It has to understand what comes after and what comes before. That's not necessarily captured in language, and all the language in the world won't capture that. One of the things that are very hard, as you mentioned, compliance is hard because that's where machines meet society and humans. Episode 48: Dmitry Balyasny - Managing Partner and CIO of Balyasny Asset Management

TL;DR Dmitry Balyasny, Founder and CIO of Balyasny Asset Management (BAM), discusses his journey from an immigrant to building a leading $30B multi-strategy hedge fund, emphasizing a disciplined business model, partnership culture, continuous talent development, and adaptable...

TL;DR Dmitry Balyasny, Founder and CIO of Balyasny Asset Management (BAM), discusses his journey from an immigrant to building a leading $30B multi-strategy hedge fund, emphasizing a disciplined business model, partnership culture, continuous talent development, and adaptable edge to generate consistent returns across market cycles.

Dmitry Balyasny's Formative Journey

  • Early Life & Immigration: Dmitry Balyasny immigrated from Kiev, Ukraine to Chicago at age 7, speaking no English. His parents, a professor and engineer, bravely restarted their lives in menial jobs, instilling resilience.
      • His personal experience involved moving from 3rd to 2nd grade due to language barriers, highlighting initial challenges.
  • Entrepreneurial Spirit: From junior high, he pursued sales jobs for commission, quickly recognizing it as a path to earning money as a kid.
  • Early Foray into Finance: Became a stockbroker in college to fund personal trading, where he initially lost all earnings and more, leveraging with credit cards due to lack of mentorship and understanding of proper trading.
      • Persistence: Despite significant early losses, his passion for markets and a belief that consistent effort with reasonable instruction could lead to competence kept him going.
  • Impact of "Atlas Shrugged": Reading Ayn Rand's "Atlas Shrugged" between high school and college deeply resonated with his views on freedom, independence, self-determination, and the moral benefit of individual self-interest and earned rewards for society.
  • Start at Schonfeld Securities (1994): Joined after persevering through numerous rejections, attracted by their willingness to teach new traders without preconceived notions. Started with no salary and minimal capital, learning disciplined trading and risk management.
      • Career Progression: Steadily improved P&L, eventually hiring and leading a team of traders and later, fundamental analysts and sector portfolio managers, before spinning off his group.

The Genesis and Evolution of Balyasny Asset Management

  • Lessons from Schonfeld:
      • Disciplined Approach: Emphasized the need for a specific, well-thought-out trading approach with defined limits, risk profiles, and continuous iteration for improvement.
      • Fund-of-Funds Experience: Allocating firm capital revealed a critical distinction: "boutique" structures centered on individual risk-takers had high turnover, while "business" models (like multi-strategy funds) offered stability, consistency, and the ability to reinvent and scale. This reinforced his vision to build a sustainable business.
  • Founding BAM (2001): Balyasny co-founded BAM with a clear conviction to build a diversified, scalable multi-strategy business, rather than a single-manager model.
      • Initial Challenges: This model was difficult to raise capital for due to its complexity compared to simpler single-manager structures, leading to significant AUM volatility in early years.
  • Key Decisions Shaping BAM:
      • Firm Architecture: Maintained unwavering conviction in the multi-strategy, multi-risk taker model, developing fund, risk, and recruiting structures around this foundation.
      • Partnership Culture: Established a strong partnership culture early on with key hires (Scott and Taylor), which now permeates the firm with 20 partners across business and investment roles. This fosters a team environment where individuals are motivated for both personal success and the firm's overall well-being.
  • Resilience Through Adversity: BAM successfully navigated challenging periods like the 2008 financial crisis (making a small profit but facing 50% redemptions) and a down year in 2018, demonstrating the strength of its culture in retaining talent and rebuilding.

Cultivating and Developing Top Investment Talent

  • Analyst vs. PM Qualities:
      • Commonality: Both require the ability to think differently, generate unique variant ideas, and balance creativity with investment process discipline.
      • Key Differences for PMs: Portfolio Managers need higher risk tolerance (innate but trainable), the ability to juggle numerous issues daily, and to balance deep dives into specific ideas with a zoomed-out view of the overall portfolio.
  • Attracting Talent: Beyond competitive compensation, BAM differentiates by offering:
      • Centralized Resources: Strategy, policy, economics research, quantitative analysis, and collaboration across diverse asset classes (equities, credit, macro, commodities, quant).
      • Supportive Coaching & Partnership: A mentality of joint venture, providing resources, coaching, and team development to ensure PM success, rather than a "good luck" approach.
  • Talent Development Programs:
      • Campus Recruiting: Extensive program for summer interns (100+ annually), with over 50% hired into various firm roles.
      • Analyst Training: Primarily a "Jedi relationship" with their PM, supplemented by central off-sites, panels, and recorded sessions sharing diverse experiences and techniques.
      • PM Training: For high-performing analysts/traders, allocating capital and teaching team/risk management aspects they may lack.
  • Uncoachable Aspects: Innate risk personality and passion for the business are difficult to fundamentally change; screening for these is crucial.
  • Culture & Returns: A strong culture is vital for both defense and offense.
      • Defensively: Enables the firm to weather difficult periods without significant talent loss or permanent impairment.
      • Offensively: Amplifies returns through seamless collaboration, sharing insights, and fostering a "flywheel" effect across teams and strategies.
  • Communication in a Specialized Model: BAM's specialized structure, where teams have distinct mandates with minimal overlap, allows for open communication without excessive unintended correlation or groupthink. Diverse perspectives from PMs often lead to different conclusions, and shared convictions are seen as strong signals.

The Evolving Edge in Hedge Funds and Leadership at BAM

  • Raising Quality Capital: Dmitry highlights the rigorous and lengthy due diligence processes of large, high-quality capital allocators (e.g., a 17-year process for a sovereign wealth fund).
      • Challenge of Maintaining Capital: It's relatively easy to raise *some* capital, but maintaining and growing it through market ups and downs requires a steadfast edge and strong culture.
  • Defining "Edge": Edge depends on an individual's skill set, which could be:
      • A great manager, recruiter, risk manager, and capital allocator who attracts talent.
      • A great trader/PM with acumen in a specific area, supported by a strong team and infrastructure.
      • Problems arise when a firm's structure doesn't match its core edge or the evolving skill set of its leadership.
  • Dmitry Balyasny's Day-to-Day as CEO/CIO:
      • Market & Risk Focus: Daily engagement with market volatility, risk management, and capital allocation, especially during busy periods (e.g., earnings, commodity volatility).
      • Strategy Development: Extensive time dedicated to planning for the firm's future across different strategy verticals, including competition analysis, infrastructure needs, and scaling.
      • Infrastructure: Involved in technology and trading systems improvements to enable strategy execution.
      • Recruiting: Consistently conducts an average of "a couple interviews every day" for PMs, business managers, and senior department roles, crucial for growth.
  • Advice to a 16-Year-Old (Carpe Diem): "Seize the day." Balance what you want to do with what you have to do, creating space for passionate pursuits and setting yourself up for a future free of regret.

Notable Quotes

Here's my limits... and have like prescribed method to the madness." — Dmitry Balyasny "If the main thing that you're offering is a, you know, sign on or a guarantee uh check uh like that's not like really compelling because it is fairly commoditized... you really have to differentiate in my opinion, you know, beyond that." — Dmitry Balyasny "If you don't really love what you're doing, you're not excited, you know, you're you're going to kind of like either actually give up or psychologically kind of kind of go through the motions but not really, you know, do everything that you can." — Dmitry Balyasny "A lot of it is uh you know market oriented, risk oriented, capital allocation oriented... This week on Generating Alpha, I sat down with Dmitri Balazni, founder, managing partner, and chief investment officer of Balazni Asset Management, one of the most respected multi-strategy hedge funds in the world. Dmitri immigrated to the United States from Kiev, Ukraine at age seven, speaking no English. He worked as a door-to-door salesman at 12, became a stockbroker in college, and started his trading career at Schonfeld Securities in 1994. In 2001, he co-founded Balazni Asset Management, which has grown to over $30 billion in assets under management with 2,300 employees across 23 global offices. Beyond building one of the industry's leading multi-manager platforms, Dmitri is the only founder among the big four multi-strat funds who still actively manages a book. He is also deeply committed to expanding opportunity, founding Atlas Fellows in 2021 to provide under-resourced young people with pathways into finance and investing. In our conversation, we talked about immigrating to America and learning to trade, the formative impact of *Atlas Shrugged*, losing everything his first year as a broker and why he persisted, what he learned at Schonfeld about building great hedge funds, the critical decisions that shaped BAM's evolution, what separates great analysts from great PMs, how culture translates into returns, the changing nature of edge, and much more. During your time in college, if I am correct, you became a broker because ultimately you wanted to become a trader, but the broker was the first step to doing that. You made some money from commissions being a broker, and if I am correct, you did not make any money in the end because you traded them and lost a lot of the money. "Yes, I was definitely not up. The career trajectory was, I did some sales starting in junior high and then throughout high school, different types of sales jobs, because I pretty quickly figured out that was the only way you could get paid any money as a kid; you had to earn commissions. Podcasting had not been invented yet. I was not smart enough to start a VC fund. So I did different types of sales. As I was looking to get into finance and trading, it was natural that I could get a foot in the door through sales, which is what being a stockbroker was at the time. You got a stack of leads to call and people you were trying to get to open accounts and do some trades or investments with the firm. I was pretty good at that, and I pretty quickly had a number of other young people working under my umbrella. We were doing very well for our age in terms of generating revenues, but I was really interested in trading, so I was taking all the commission earnings that I was making and using that to fund my personal trading account. There I had no idea what I was doing, so I was losing that and more because I was young and silly and leveraged that via credit cards. So that was my early version of prime brokerage, which was not the way to do it. I did not know what I was doing, and so I was losing more than I was making and got to a spot where I really figured out that I needed to get some formal mentorship to really figure out how to trade properly." And then you saw the only ad that Schonfeld has ever run in a newspaper, if I am correct. I am also just interested, first of all, tell me about your start at Schonfeld and your trajectory there before spinning out to BAM. I would love to hear that. But even before that, most people who lost a lot of money trading early on might have been, "Okay, this is not for me." Why did you keep on going? "Well, one, I really loved it. I was doing it not just to make money. I was doing it because I was really passionate about trading and investing and markets and companies, and I was always very interested in it. So, that was one. Two, I had never really been naturally good at anything. So I have had to pretty much work at anything that I become competent at. I usually have to spend a lot of effort to get there." I can relate. "Right. And so, I figured because I had that backbone from working my way up in other things, whether it was sales or sports or whatever, I figured if I really loved it, it is not rocket science. If I worked at it hard enough and got some reasonable instruction, I could eventually figure it out step by step. So that is where that confidence came from." And tell me about that. How did you get started at Schonfeld, and you had a very interesting career trajectory there, all the way from starting just yourself, then you ended up running a team and even on the fund-of-funds side? How did those steps lead up to spinning out of that and starting BAM? "Yes, I guess first off, just the perseverance in getting the job, I think, is important. At the time, there were not many different hedge funds to talk to, certainly not in Chicago, and I did not really have any experience that was useful for them to be interested. So I cold-called and sent resumes and worked with as many as I could find. I had a very large stack of rejection letters. But I would go through the paper every week, looking for ads for trading positions. Back in the day, that is amazingly what people did. If I had not been doing that, I would not have actually seen the one ad that ran at the time. So that was helpful. They were looking for people that did not have a lot of trading experience but had a little bit of market knowledge. They wanted to teach them the approach that they thought worked and not have people come in with preconceived notions. So I was very clear that I had no idea what worked, and I wanted to learn. I was going to work hard. And so I was hired into the training program. We had no compensation, no salary. Today there are lots of signing bonuses and multi-year guarantees and all this and that. We got free lunch, and that was it. Then you got a percentage of the profits that you generated from your trading account, but you had a very small pool of capital to start because the firm was trading its own capital, and it made sense to start people with a small amount of capital when they did not know what they were doing. So the first year I did not make anything there, and there were some people there that did not make anything for years and eventually dropped out. But I persevered and kept asking questions and kept slowly improving and learning different disciplines and types of trading and types of risk management that worked, initially in equities and then broadening it out to macro, et cetera. After the first year, I steadily started improving and consistently putting up solid P&L. As that grew, I started hiring people to work under my profit and loss center. Initially, I hired traders who I basically taught what I knew and gave them some responsibility to take a portion of my risk in different sectors. Then I started hiring analysts, five or six years into it. We were the first group in the firm to hire fundamental analysts, which was an unusual thing to do in the nineties because it was such a momentum market. But I thought that was not going to last forever, and if I wanted to keep scaling and growing, we needed to do fundamental work and hold positions longer. So we started doing that, and then that morphed into hiring sector portfolio managers where we gave them more capital to manage under my umbrella. Then that became the genesis of our trading group, and as we grew and expanded, we wound up spinning it off into a division and then into a separate fund entity that we capitalized." And I am interested in what you learned at Schonfeld, both from building a team over the years, but also, if I am correct, you were on the fund-of-funds side for a bit and got to interact with some of the top funds at the time. What did you learn there about what makes a great hedge fund, and how did you apply those principles to the beginnings of BAM? "Yes, that is a good question. On the trading side, I would say having a disciplined, specific approach. The markets have lots of things going on all the time. There are a million different types of trading and investing that you could do in different asset classes, different holding periods: long, short, neutral, investing, trading. So there are a million different things you could do. Generally, people do not really have a particular, well-thought-out approach. They do something, it works, they do a little more of it, it does not work, they do something else, they try this, they try that. That is what I was doing before. You really need a particular approach: this is the type of trade that I look for, and this is the type of risk that I take. Here are my limits. Here is what I am trying to make, here is what I am limiting myself to lose, and have a prescribed method to the madness. You start with a fairly small box. When we start new portfolio managers who had not run money before, we try to make the box fairly tight. When I started, it was really tight because we really did not have much experience. Today, the people we have starting off in a PM training program already have a lot of experience, so they have a much wider box. But you need that box so that it says, 'Here is the stuff that I think I have an edge in this particular portion of the market. Here is how long I typically hold positions. Here is the factor risk I want to take or not take. Here is the concentration, the liquidity, how much I am trying to make, how much I am willing to lose as a drawdown.' So you define all this stuff, and then as you get competent, the more success you have, you gradually widen out that box. Sometimes you grow it, and then you have a drawdown, something changes in the market, and you have to shrink it again and go back to your basics a little bit. But over time, that is how you develop, and then you are constantly looking for improvements in your approach, not just what happened with this particular trade, but how can I use what I learned so that my next thousand trades are better? So, you are constantly iterating a little bit. From the fund-of-funds side, I was allocating a firm account that Steve Schonfeld capitalized, and I co-invested with a couple of other guys. I was the fund-of-fund analyst. I went around and met a bunch of different funds to help pick where we should be invested. That was a really interesting experience. I would say the main thing that was really reinforced to me that was helpful was that there is a huge difference between two types of funds. One is a boutique structure that is structured around an individual risk-taker in a particular strategy." Yes. "And that is the vast majority of hedge funds. And then there is a business. It could be a proprietary trading business, like a Jane Street or HRT, or it could be a hedge fund with a lot of different risk-takers. That is a completely different DNA from the individual-centered model. What I saw, as we were allocating the portfolio, was split between the two models. We had a lot of different funds. The single manager model had a tremendous amount of turnover. Some did great, then they did not do great, and the manager would want to do something else or lose motivation or had some business issues. But it was very rare that we were invested with the same guys for 10 or 20 years. In that part of the book, they just turned over a lot. Versus the diversified funds, a lot of them ended up being the same ones for decades, or they would spin off, and one would become two, but it was the same basic DNA. The reason for that is that type of firm can reinvent itself. You can expand different strategies, some work, some do not, you change the mix of strategies, you can allocate capital to the areas of the market where you think there are better opportunities, you can refresh your investing teams as people develop and get better or not over time. So it is just a much more stable and consistent business model. So that reinforced that that was the way that I wanted to go in really building out our business." Yes, you wanted to build a business and a legacy rather than something more fleeting. "Yes, I just wanted to build a real business. So whether I had a good year trading or not, I wanted the firm to do well. I wanted our investors to do well, and I wanted to do well in all market environments. Some are great for my style of trading, some are not. I wanted it to be a consistent, scalable business." Yes. I would love to hear a little bit about, before we touch on talent and different topics, I would love to hear a bit about, from a high-level perspective, how BAM has evolved over the past 25 years. I would love to hear what you think were the most important decisions that led to it becoming what it is today. "Well, I think the first one is just what I was talking about: what is the architecture? What are you trying to build? A lot of times when people start funds, they start from the standpoint of how much money can I raise? What will people invest in? You obviously have to think about that, but that should not be the genesis of what you are trying to build, because investor appetites change, and they certainly will follow returns. You need something that you really believe in over good periods, bad periods, that you have a particular edge in and you know how to do, and architect the firm towards that, regardless if it is easy or harder to raise money for it to start. The reason I say that is this model was really hard to raise money for. We had a lot of ups and downs over the years in AUM before we got to the real critical mass and a really solid investor base. That took a long time, and the reason for it is it is a complicated model for an investor to understand. If you compare it to an individual single-manager PM coming in and saying, 'Here is my track record. You are investing with me. I am great at XYZ. I have this team. We have five guys. We worked together for 10 years. Simple fee structure, simple risk. Here it is.' Versus we come in and we are like, 'Well, the PMs change. We have a hundred different people; today we have 200 different PMs. There are a couple thousand people, all these different strategies; they are going to evolve over time. Everything is complicated.' So it takes investors a long time to get comfortable with that. But really having conviction that that is the model that I wanted to pursue was super important because that conviction gets tested. A lot of people want to do a particular model, and then when times get tough, they are like, 'Maybe I will do something else.' We really architected that we wanted to build a really diverse, scalable business, a real multi-risk or multi-strategy, and we stuck with it. We created a fund structure, risk structure, recruiting structure, everything built around that foundation. So, I would say that is the first critical thing because it is very hard to veer from a single manager to then say, 'Okay, I am going to have lots of different risk-takers, and now we are going to do multi-manager.' That is very, very hard. So I think that has been critical to success, just that steadfastness. Then a partnership culture is really important. I was fortunate enough to hire Scott and Taylor as two partners very early on, and we started the business together. So we have been together for 25 years, and that partnership culture permeates the firm. Now we have 20 partners, both strategy heads on the business side, department heads, and portfolio managers as well. So that really feeds the team-type environment where people want the firm to do well and are rooting for the overall firm, in addition to obviously being very motivated and the economics that they get from generating P&L on their own portion of capital that they are managing. So I think combining those two things is very hard to do, and most firms are kind of one or the other, and we have always believed you could be both. You could have individual incentives, and you could have firm incentives and firm partnership, and those things really reinforce each other. So I think those are the main ones."
