Thank you. And back to the episode. Yes. And I would love to touch on the topic of people. I think obviously what makes these firms enduring is a consistent flow of great talent. So I am interested, just to start off, what makes a great analyst and what makes a great PM, and how do those differ? "Yes, good question. What makes a great analyst, and I think this is similar to a great PM, is you have to be able to think differently. You have to come up with unique, variant ideas, so differing views on things that lots of other people are constantly looking at. You might not have a differing view necessarily for the next five years, but you should be able to come up with a consistent flow of differing views for the next few months, maybe the next year, maybe the next couple of years in some cases. So that takes a particular type of wiring where you are constantly looking at the other side of the coin and what might cause people to come around to your view and then digging on that. So the balance of creativity and investment process discipline is common for both analysts, and you need that for PMs as well. A lot of times people that are really creative and come up with brilliant ideas do not have process discipline. They might fall in love with one particular idea. They have a hard time running models on a hundred different companies. You need both things. So that is common. I think the thing that is different is at a PM level, you certainly need more risk tolerance, where you are comfortable with volatility, and some of that is innate and some of it can be learned. You need the ability to juggle lots of different balls. As an analyst, the great thing about being an analyst is you can go super deep on a particular thing and not worry about anything else for some period of time. If it is a big enough idea, you can raise your hand, talk to your PM, and say, 'Listen, I think we should spend the next week, I am just going to work on this.' And if you agree that this is a big potential thing, 'I am going to spend 90% of my time digging on this until we either prove or disprove this thesis because I think this could be a really big bet.' As a PM, you do not have that luxury. Every day there is something blowing up in your book. Hopefully, there is something well going on. There is some macro problem, somebody tweeted something. There are constantly 50 different things that you are juggling. So the ability to do deep work and dig in on something but also to zoom out and look at the overall portfolio and constantly balance those things, that is a unique PM skill." And on the topic of talent, in recent years, a lot of people have seen these big headlines of hedge funds paying up for big talent. But I do think that at a point, the marginal compensation does not really matter as much as the type of firm you will be going to. An extra million dollars on a $100 million contract is not going to matter as much as the firm. So my question to you regarding that is how do you uniquely position yourselves to attract the best talent as a firm? "Yes. So I think once you are at the level economically where you can compete, and realistically 99% of funds do not really compete in this pool for actual portfolio managers running meaningful books and getting paid on their performance because they do not have the economic ability and scale and structure to compete for that. So once you are past that and you are competing with other large firms in that space, if the main thing that you are offering is a sign-on or a guarantee check, that is not really compelling because it is fairly commoditized. If you are a strong PM, you have a good track record, you are a good team manager, you are a good risk manager, maybe somebody needs that particular coverage area more than somebody else, but in general, you are going to get paid very well if you go to any of the top firms. So you really have to differentiate, in my opinion, beyond that. You see this all the time in sports, and I do think it is somewhat similar. Athletes certainly want security and to get paid for their talent, rightly so. But you also frequently see them taking a smaller check or certainly a smaller guarantee to go on a winning team, or to go play with guys where they want to be on that team or play for a particular coach, and have the ability to bet on themselves and bet on that team, and then if it works out, they will hopefully end up doing even better financially. You see that all the time. So I do not think it is that different. So we try, beyond the basic economics of being one of the larger places that can offer that, beyond that, can you offer the central resources that they might not have somewhere? The strategy research, policy research, economics research, collaboration across equities, credit, macro, commodities, quant—all these areas where guys are constantly talking to each other and comparing notes of what is going on in the macro, what is going on in the market, what is working. Having great QR functions that work with the teams to optimize their risk-taking, to give them suggestions statistically on what works, what does not work for them, for others. And then just having a very supportive coaching mentality and a partnership mentality where we are going into this venture together. We are both making a bet, just like venture capital, we are both making a bet, and we are going to do everything possible to make the team successful. Not to say that if somebody loses money for years, we are not going to give up and make a change; we have to be disciplined on that, we will. But before that, we are going to do everything possible to make the team successful in terms of resources, coaching, team development, anything that we can do to help them get to where they want to go, as opposed to, 'Here is your capital, here is your risk, good luck. If it works out, great. If not, we will get somebody else.'" And on the topic of playing for a good coach, there is this idea of coaching and developing talent. Over the past year, I have gotten to know you guys and your campus recruiting program quite well, but there is obviously the campus recruiting program and then all the way up to the PM coaching program. From a high level, how do you internally develop talent from analysts to PMs? And what cannot be coached is also a question I have. "I think risk is tough to coach. I think people have an innate risk personality, and I think you can influence it, but it is hard to move it a lot. So you just have to go into it with eyes wide open that somebody that is super conservative, you are never going to get them to really be aggressive. And somebody that is super aggressive, you are never going to get them to be conservative. So wherever they are, you can help modulate them a little bit, and hopefully you can help the offensive person play a little better defense and the defensive person play a little bit more aggressively when opportunities are there. You are not going to hugely move them. So that is an innate psychology thing that you have to be comfortable with in the screening process. We look for passion a lot: is the person super excited to be in this business, in this firm, in this role, because these are hard? There are huge ups and downs. There are miserable periods when nothing is working, you feel like the biggest idiot in the world, you cannot put two cents together. Everyone goes through those periods. And if you do not really love what you are doing, you are not excited, you are going to either actually give up or psychologically go through the motions but not really do everything that you can. So those are a couple of things that we do. The different programs: we have everything starting from a college recruiting program where we have kids come in from all kinds of top schools and investment clubs and math programs, and they will go through a summer internship that is structured, and we will typically hire or make offers to about 65% of those kids. We end up hiring over 50% of the interns every year. I think this last year we had over 100 interns. I think this next year is probably going to be 130, 140 globally. So that is fun, exciting, and they take jobs throughout the firm: some in technology, some in risk, some in investing, some in trading. Then you have analyst training programs. As an analyst, the majority of your training should come from your portfolio manager. It is a bit of a Jedi relationship that you are signing up for. So you have to like and respect your Jedi and think that that is somebody you could learn a lot from, and vice versa. And that is the primary one, but in addition to that, we try to do a lot of stuff centrally because we have a lot of Jedi. So you can pick up different things from different people, different strengths, different techniques, different strategies. And so we will do central things, whether it is an off-site where different PMs will be on panels talking to the analysts, dinners, in-office gatherings, all sorts of stuff where some formal, informal, where somebody will talk about their experience, examples, and you go through this formal knowledge transfer, not just from the direct person you are working for, but throughout the firm. Then we try to put all these in a recorded format so people can get the benefit of all the sessions that have been done prior, and so you have this amazing encyclopedia that you could go through and hopefully learn from other people's mistakes and avoid having to make all of them yourself. Then you have the PM training program where somebody who is typically a great analyst or a great trader already, and now wants to move up to the PM seat, and there we will allocate capital to them, help them build out the team, and work to teach them the portions of being a PM that they might not know. So they might be great at picking stocks, but maybe they have not managed a team before, maybe they have not managed risk before. So there is a lot of both formal and informal training around those." And I picked up on something you said, which is when you were describing the departments, you said there is investing and there is trading. A lot of people see the overlap of investing and trading as the same thing. What is the difference between investing and trading to you? "Well, it is a little bit technical, and it depends a bit on the strategy. In macro, for example, when you say somebody is a good trader, it is typically the same thing as a good portfolio manager; they are running. Portfolio tends to be a very trading-oriented strategy. When you look at equities, they are different titles. So somebody today who is a trader in equities, they are typically on the trading desk, working orders, helping optimize execution, helping the portfolio manager trade the portfolio. And the portfolio manager is typically a little bit more fundamental, usually started off as an analyst, although we have some that started off as traders, and they are digging through the research with the analyst trying to pick the best investment ideas. So, it depends a little bit on the strategy." And you cannot talk about people without talking about culture. So, how does culture translate all the way from the culture in the building to returns for your investors? "I think it is all important. So many examples. One is, when you go through a really difficult period, if you do not have a strong culture internally, often businesses fold, or if they do not fold, they get pretty permanently impaired. You see that a lot, whether it is public companies, and you trade their stock, and you look at how companies recover from difficult periods or do not, and the list of top companies is hugely different every decade. Hedge funds, if you look at the top funds 10 years ago, 20 years ago, there is not a lot of overlap. There is some, typically like the multi-strategy firms, a few others, but there is huge change. A big reason for that is there is not enough that people are buying into beyond their current job for most funds. If you are tied a little bit more to the business and you really want the firm to do well, in addition to obviously wanting to progress in your own job and do great, when you want the person next to you to do well and the firm to do well, the whole thing is just much more solid, a lot more cohesive, and it is a lot stronger and able to get through all the ups and downs. So, whether you look at a period like 2008, where we ended up making a little bit of money, but we had 50% redemptions because people needed cash from other places, and we paid it out. We had a big headcount and had to cough up the difference from the management company. Oftentimes when people want to start funds, they are always talking about how much they are going to make on management fees. 90% of the time we lose money on management fees, and a couple of those years we had to put up a lot of money on the management fee side to get the business through it. But because of the culture, we did not lose many people. Same thing, we had one down year in 2018 out of 25 years. Again, we lost a lot of AUM, had to do a lot of restructuring. We lost a few people, but not many. I think most firms, if you do not have a strong culture, it is very hard to get through that. Another side of the coin is the offensive side. When things are going well, I think culture also really helps you amplify the returns, because if you do not really care how the person next to you does or how the overall firm does, you are not really going to collaborate with them to make them better, whether it is an investing insight that you could share that does not cost you anything but will help them. Or it is a process insight, your analyst is unhappy, 'I heard him talking by the water cooler. Maybe you should take him out to dinner and see if you guys get on the same page.' It could be a process insight on, 'Here is this new trick I learned in AI that is super useful for my investment process, maybe you should try this.' There are a million things like that every day, but you need a culture for that to permeate the firm, and when it does, and you can permeate that across teams, across asset classes, across strategies, everyone just becomes much better faster. You get the flywheel going. So I think it is super important, both defensively and offensively." And I am interested in this aspect of communication because there are some multi-managers that infamously are known to be very not communicative. So you seem to have a very communicative culture. Why did you choose to have that, and what do you think your upside to that is? "So there is no perfect system. There is the right thing for different firms of what you are trying to accomplish, but it has to fit with the architecture of the firm. So, to me, if you have a generalist mandate type of place where people can trade a lot of different things and there is a lot of overlap between what different people are doing, in that case, you really have to be very careful about the communication because you could end up with a lot of unintended correlation. So that is one model. We have always driven towards a specialized model. So we do not have very many pockets where there is a lot of overlap. You will have people that have a particular equity sector or geography that they trade, or a particular macro mandate that they trade, a particular commodity product that they trade. And we do not hire many, many people to trade the same types of things. We do a lot of analysis of what is the alpha pool in this particular strategy, how many teams do we need to cover that, what is the rough optimum number? And then we adjust that over time. So you want to get to a spot where there is enough coverage to pick up a lot of the alpha at scale, but not so much that you are creating a lot of friction and preventing people from collaborating. If you have that, and you do not have any particular team managing too much of the overall risk and having too much overlap with anybody else, then I think the risks of them talking to each other are much lower than the benefits you get from that. There is a preconception that if three PMs talk about an idea, they are all going to agree and have groupthink and go do the same thing. Most of the time, what actually happens is they will end up arguing for an hour and have three completely different views, and if they could have three different positions, they will have three different positions. And if they all actually do agree on something and come at it from different research and different styles and come to the same conclusion, very often that is a pretty good signal that you have a good idea. So I think the benefits of it really outweigh the downside with the type of firm architecture that we have, and it certainly has helped our returns over time." And I am very interested in this idea of, if I wanted to raise a hedge fund and I went out today and I pitched a sovereign wealth fund or I pitched a pension, one of the questions they would probably ask me is, "What is your edge?" How have you seen the nature of edge within the hedge fund industry change over the last two decades as a whole? What has it become? "Well, first of all, I think there is different quality, I would say, of capital that you can raise. When you say, 'I want to go pitch a sovereign wealth fund,' to give you a sense, we had a sovereign wealth fund invest fairly recently, and the first time I met with them was 17 years ago. So that was the due diligence period. Now, somebody else might be a lot smarter than me, maybe it will not take them 17 years, but the high-quality, large pools of capital are pretty rigorous on what they want to see and how long they want to see it for before they start writing meaningful checks most of the time. So that is one of the things that makes starting hedge funds very difficult: the quality of the capital that you typically get starting off, and certainly a year, two years, three years into it, it is very volatile. So if you go through a difficult period, it comes back to that culture and edge. Can you maintain it? It is much less about, 'Can you raise the capital?' It is not that hard to raise some capital, but maintaining it and growing it through the ups and downs, that is what is hard. In terms of what edge to demonstrate, there are different approaches, and it really depends on the person's skill set. So somebody could be a great manager; they are a great recruiter, great manager, great risk manager, capital allocator, and they have an ability to attract talent to them in the space that they really know well. So that is one model. Another model is somebody is a great trader PM in a particular area, and people are going to invest with them for their acumen in that particular thing, and they surround themselves with a strong team and strong infrastructure for that particular thing and get the right amount of capital for the capacity that they can generate good returns on and try to maintain and slowly grow from that. I think people run into problems when they veer from that core edge, and the structure of the firm does not match with their skill set as much. So if somebody is a really good risk-taker in a particular thing, that does not necessarily make them a great talent manager, recruiter, business builder. They might be, but most of the time they are not. So you have to match that up. I think that is really important, especially as the firm evolves, and if that person does not have it, then bringing in people that really have a different skill set, and that is one of the areas that I was fortunate in, and we have always invested a lot in bringing in top management and continuing to really do that, get things going, and then bring in folks to take it to the next level." And I think a lot of people have this question in their minds: people know what analysts do, people do not know what PMs do, but people do not really know what the heads of these large multi-manager hedge funds do. So my question to you, before I ask the final question I ask every guest, is, as the CEO and CIO of a $30 billion hedge fund, what does a day in the life of Dmitri look like? "Well, today was a very busy earnings day. So we are dealing with all sorts of earnings volatility and trading pretty actively, talking to the teams about different ideas. The last few weeks there has been crazy volatility in the commodities market. So I spent a lot of time talking to the management team, the risk team, the PMs about what is going on in their space, how can we manage through it well, and hopefully add or at least not subtract value through that process, figure out where you need to protect capital and where you need to lean in on stuff. So a lot of it is market-oriented, risk-oriented, capital allocation-oriented. So that is every day. Then the other big part is strategy development. We split our business up into different strategy verticals, and for each one, we spent a lot of time thinking about where are we today, where do we want to be tomorrow, where do we want to be three, four, five years from now, how do we get there? How do you compete? How do you win? Where can you build an edge? What kind of infrastructure do you need to build to get to the next level? Who can scale? How do we resource them for that? Where do we need to hire? So there is a lot of strategy development, and that takes some business acumen but also market acumen to try to decipher between things that might be fleeting and things that might be enduring strategies that you want to invest in." So, like trades versus businesses, we spent a lot of time on that in investment committee and in conversations with the management team and senior PMs. The infrastructure piece is increasingly important. I was in a technology meeting earlier, a trading technology meeting, discussing our trading systems, how we want to improve them, the targets we want to enable for various strategies, and what we're doing about it. Some time is spent on that. Another large piece is recruiting – portfolio managers, business managers, and senior department folks in different areas. I would say I do an average of probably a couple interviews every day. In some parts of the year, it could be much more than that. Even if I'm not the one doing the interview, I'm triangulating with the hiring manager for that role and our business development team, figuring out if this sounds like the right person, what we're looking for, and how we get this done. That's a super important part of the business if you want to grow. I want to finish off by asking my final question, which I ask every guest. I'm 16 right now. If you were to give one piece of advice to a 16-year-old today, it can be life advice, career advice. I'm pretty sure Jeff Yas gave me romantic advice. What would yours be? Oh yeah, I heard that one. That's pretty good, actually. That's good advice. Yeah, what would yours be? Any kind. I think one that I think about a lot is Carpe Diem, seize the day. You have to constantly have a balance between things that you want to be doing and things that you have to be doing, and that never goes away. I'm not saying don't do anything that you don't absolutely want to do at that moment. There are lots of things you have to do to get to where you want to get to. But making sure you're spending enough time doing the things that you really want to do and creating space for that. Then thinking ahead about what you really want to do, setting yourself up, and doing the things you have to do to get there, so that you don't spend too many hours or years doing things that you look back on later as a waste. Live with no regrets. Yes. Well, it's been a pleasure, Demetri. I'm glad we could make this happen. Thanks for coming on. My pleasure. Let's chat with him.

"Everything has changed" - Gita Gopinath on the global economy in 2026

World Economic Forum(1.1M) ·28:05 ·11.1K views · 2026-02-05
Gita Gopinath Keynote
Despite stable global growth numbers in 2025, Harvard economist Gita Gopinath warns that the global economy has fundamentally shifted, moving away from a predictable order due to escalating tariffs, a breakdown of trust between major alliances, and an AI boom whose financial valuations carry significant systemic risk.
TL;DR Despite stable global growth numbers in 2025, Harvard economist Gita Gopinath warns that the global economy has fundamentally shifted, moving away from a predictable order due to escalating tariffs, a breakdown of trust between major alliances, and an AI boom whose...

TL;DR

Despite stable global growth numbers in 2025, Harvard economist Gita Gopinath warns that the global economy has fundamentally shifted, moving away from a predictable order due to escalating tariffs, a breakdown of trust between major alliances, and an AI boom whose financial valuations carry significant systemic risk. This profound uncertainty makes the economy far more fragile than headline figures suggest.

The Disconnect: Stable Growth Amidst Fundamental Change

  • Gita Gopinath's Core Message: The global economy has undergone fundamental changes, even though 2025 growth (3.2%) remained stable, masking underlying fragility.
  • Initial Tariff Impact: The US imposed some of its highest tariff rates in a century (actual paid rates around 14%, statutory 22-23%, up from 3%), yet anticipated severe economic consequences did not materialize.
  • Reasons for Mitigated Tariff Impact:
      • Paused Tariffs: Initial harsh tariffs were paused for negotiation, preventing an immediate shock.
      • Exemptions & Lag: Numerous exemptions for specific products and countries, alongside a grace period for goods already in transit, lowered the effective tariff burden.
  • Economic Offsets: Two key factors prevented a downturn:
      • AI Boom: Massive investment in Artificial Intelligence fueled a stock market surge, boosting wealth and consumption, particularly at higher income levels. Countries supplying AI inputs (e.g., Taiwan, South Korea) saw significant growth.
      • Fiscal Stimulus: Strong government spending in China, Germany (moving away from debt breaks), and the US (in 2026) provided crucial economic support.

Rupturing the Global Order & Rising Uncertainty

  • End of Predictable Trade: The world has moved away from a rules-based, predictable global trade order, introducing a pervasive level of uncertainty affecting businesses, especially smaller ones.
  • US-Europe Alliance Breakdown: A critical and unforeseen development is the "complete breakdown of trust" between the US and Europe, rupturing an alliance that was a cornerstone of the global economic order for 80 years.
      • Europe is now actively pursuing "strategic autonomy," seeking reduced reliance on the US for security and economic partnership, and forging new trade alliances (e.g., Mercosur).
  • Slow-Burn Effects: Similar to Brexit, the full economic consequences of these fundamental changes (e.g., on investment) are expected to unfold gradually over several years, rather than immediately.
  • Accelerated Geopolitical Risks: The current geopolitical landscape is "profound for at least our generation," marking a "once-in-a-century event" due to the breakdown of the global order, a significant departure from the post-Cold War peace dividend.

Key Risks to Global Stability

  • AI Bubble & Financial Fragility: The AI sector, a major growth driver, exhibits stretched valuations and significant potential for a correction.
      • Dot-Com Bust Comparison: A potential "dot-com-like bust" in AI could wipe out $35 trillion in wealth, far exceeding the original dot-com crash and representing a much larger share of current global GDP.
      • Profitability Concerns: Gopinath questions whether all AI companies can achieve the profits necessary to justify their current high valuations in a hyper-competitive market.
      • Financial Crisis Risk: Despite recent resilience, current easy financial conditions could be building towards a future financial crisis, potentially triggered by unexpected inflation forcing the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates, tightening financial conditions.
  • Debt Overhang: Almost all countries face record-high levels of government debt without clear paths for reduction, representing a major risk currently overshadowed by geopolitical tensions.
  • Central Bank Independence: The operational independence of central banks is crucial for maintaining low inflation and interest rates. Political pressure on institutions like the Federal Reserve poses a significant risk to this established principle.
  • Geopolitical Escalation: Threats of retaliation from the European Union (e.g., over US statements on Greenland), coupled with ongoing tensions in regions like Iran and Venezuela, risk amplifying trade wars and broader global instability.

Future Growth Drivers & Policy Outlook

  • AI's Productivity Promise: AI is expected to boost economic growth through productivity gains, though the exact timeline (a few years or longer) and magnitude (estimates range from 0.1% to 1.5% annual productivity growth) remain uncertain.
      • Job Market Concerns: Rapid AI transformation could lead to "jobless growth," a shrinking middle class, and potential social unrest if job displacement outpaces job creation.
  • Energy Transition: Investment in green energy (solar, wind, hydro) is a significant growth driver, especially for emerging and developing countries seeking energy independence, job creation, and solutions to local pollution.
  • China's Economic Challenges:
      • Projected Slowdown: China's growth is projected to slow from 5% (2025) to 4.5% (2026) and potentially 3.5% in the medium term.
      • Key Headwinds: Weak domestic consumption, an unresolved property sector crisis, an aging and shrinking population, and weak productivity growth are major challenges.
      • Required Shift: China must transition from an export-driven model to one focused on domestic demand, requiring stronger social safety nets and greater reliance on private enterprise.
  • Resurgence of Industrial Policy: Industrial policy is making a comeback globally, driven by national security concerns (e.g., chips, rare earths, defense) and reactions to existing policies in countries like China.
      • Risks: If executed poorly, industrial policy can lead to resource misallocation, stunted growth, increased national debt, and higher interest rates. Guardrails like targeted, temporary implementation are crucial.
  • 2026 Outlook: Gopinath expresses significant concern that 2026 has started with further rupturing of US-EU relations, indicating the world is "not correcting course" but rather "going off course." She hopes for de-escalation by global leaders.

Notable Quotes

"Don't be fooled. Everything has changed for the global economy. It hasn't shown up in the growth numbers for a bunch of reasons... But be aware that the world is in a very different place." – Gita Gopinath "The global economy is more fragile than headline numbers suggest." – Gita Gopinath "What's long lasting is the complete breakdown of trust between the US and the Europe. I mean that alliance was a critical part of the global economic order and the fact that that is being ruptured is very consequential." – Gita Gopinath "If you had a dot like bust, it would wipe out $35 trillion of wealth. That is many multiples of what happened during the com. It's a much bigger share of current world GDP than it was during the com. So it can be much more consequential." – Gita Gopinath
Everything has changed for the global economy. It hasn't shown up in the growth numbers for a bunch of reasons that I'm happy to get into. But be aware that the world is in a very different place. Welcome to Radio Davos, the podcast from the World Economic Forum that looks at the biggest challenges and how we might solve them. On this episode, we're looking at the state of the global economy and what to look out for in 2026. Find Radio Davos wherever you get podcasts: Spotify, Apple, YouTube, and all our podcasts are at w.chmpodcast from the World Economic Forum. This is Radio Davos. We're recording this video podcast at the annual meeting 2026 in Davos and are joined by one of the world's most distinguished economists. She's the former first deputy managing director and chief economist at the International Monetary Fund and she's now the Gregory and Anna Coffee Professor of Economics at Harvard University, Gita Gopinath. Gita, welcome to Radio Davos. It's a pleasure to join you, Robin. It's so great to have you here. You published an article in the Financial Times a few days ago, a few days before the Davos meeting started, and I'm going to quote you from it: "2025 was a year when everything changed yet somehow nothing did." What did you mean? 2025 was a year when we saw the US impose one of the highest tariff rates they'd had in over a century. They brought it down from extremely high levels, but still, the tariff rate was around 14%, much higher than at the start of the year where it was around 3%. There was also a lot of policy chaos, a lot of confusion about where the rates would settle. You would think that would have been very consequential for the global economy. But if you look at global growth, global growth for last year was 3.2%, pretty much exactly what was projected for growth in 2025, the year before when none of this was on the horizon. It's like nothing happened. The reason I wrote that piece was to basically make the point that, no, please be careful. Don't be fooled. Everything has changed for the global economy. It hasn't shown up in the growth numbers for a bunch of reasons that I'm happy to get into. But be aware that the world is in a very different place. Let's talk about the two things you mentioned then. Why didn't it have the impact that I think a lot of economists were expecting to happen? And then we'll look at what these fundamental changes are that you say. The first question then is, why did it not bring GDP growth down? There was a time last year when economists thought this could be highly consequential and even put the US economy in a recession. That was right after Liberation Day and the April 2nd tariffs. In that three-day window, markets reacted very strongly; it looked like something was going to break. But then the Trump administration basically put a pause on those tariffs and said they were opening up time for negotiation with countries. So, one important reason why things turned out better than expected was because the actual tariff rates were much lower than what was originally announced. If you look at the true tariffs being paid by firms as they're importing into the US, that's even lower than the statutory rate, which is around 22-23%. The actual tariff rate in terms of what they pay is 14%. Why is that? Why is there a discrepancy? There are a bunch of reasons for it. First, there have been a lot of exemptions provided to certain products and countries. Tariffs have been announced but then withdrawn, so the actual tariff rate is very different. Also, if your goods were on the sea when the tariff was imposed, you don't get slapped with the tariff. So, there were a lot of exemptions, and that played an important role. The economy did better than expected also because there were two important offsets to the world economy. One was AI. That was huge. There was a large amount of investment in AI. Stock markets boomed because of that, which was good for wealth and helped hold up consumption at the top end and encourage consumption at the top end of incomes. Countries that were supplying inputs into the AI boom also did really well. Exporters like Taiwan and South Korea saw their growth get a big burst because of AI. The second is fiscal policy, especially in China, where there was a lot of fiscal stimulus. Germany announced moving away from the debt break rule, and the effect of that is going to play out over this year and next year. The US economy also has a strong fiscal stimulus this particular year in 2026. All of that is providing support to an economy that's otherwise being pushed back by tariffs. So what has fundamentally changed then? I think you're saying there is still a risk to the downside for economic growth, but are there real fundamental changes that have happened last year that are going to hit us this year? The fundamental change that has happened is we've moved away from the rules-based, predictable global trade order. There is a level of uncertainty that wasn't there before. That uncertainty is affecting businesses, especially small businesses. It takes time for that effect to play out. What I'm concerned about is that last year, at least, there was no overall retaliation from all of the US's trading partners. China was the one country that retaliated; Europe did not. But we're starting this year with threats of retaliation from the European Union because of the US's statements on Greenland. That can amplify. This world, where there's so much uncertainty, makes it very hard to make investments and to do business. I like to think of what happened after Brexit. Right after Brexit, there were two years when investment continued to go up, and everybody thought this couldn't really be such a big deal. But then ultimately, investment slowed, and 10 years later, you look back and say this was really a negative effect on the UK economy. I'm not saying that's the exact effect that would be on the US because the US is a much larger economy than the UK. But still, all of these changes, not just in terms of international policy but also domestic policy, concerns about central bank independence, those have slower effects, but they do show up over time. It is an extraordinary time in terms of the speed of news breaking, where economists are going to change potentially their outlook for various economic indicators. Since you wrote that article in the Financial Times, Donald Trump announced more tariffs on Europe because of the Greenland thing. That happened between you writing that and coming here to Davos. We're recording this for anyone watching or listening on the day before Donald Trump gives his special address here in Davos, so who knows what might have happened in the interim. Anecdotally, you've been at the forefront of economic analysis for many years. Have you seen a time like this before? Is this a different scene? I was chief economist at the IMF when we had the pandemic, and that was something we hadn't seen in a century. So, that was a major shift. We couldn't be in the same room together; we had to be working remotely, so that was dramatic. Compared to that, this is somewhat less dramatic, but still, it is a once-in-a-century event with this breakdown of the global order, and we may just be at the beginning of all this happening. To your previous question about what is long-lasting, I think what's long-lasting is the complete breakdown of trust between the US and Europe. That alliance was a critical part of the global economic order, and the fact that it is being ruptured is very consequential. Maybe by the end of this Davos week and next week when this goes out, we'll know if things are being smoothed out in that relationship or if things are getting tough and there's more of a trade war. We just don't know right now. We don't know. But I do believe we are never going back to the time when Europe said they could rely completely on partnership with the US for their security and economic partnership. This has changed fundamentally. I think Europe is looking for strategic autonomy. They are figuring out how to rely less on payments dominated by US companies and, more generally, how to rely on their own internal economy and security. So, there's no going back on that regardless of how this week goes. Let's talk about economic growth. In your article, you said, and I quote, "The global economy is more fragile than headline numbers suggest." What are the main risks? You've already described the potential delayed impact of increased tariffs. Are there others? The sector that did incredibly well last year was artificial intelligence. That was a big boost to the global economy. I think that's a sector where there is still a lot of fragility. When I wrote the article, I wrote an article last year in The Economist around October where I said that if you had a dot-com-like bust, it would wipe out $35 trillion of wealth. That is many multiples of what happened during the dot-com era. It's a much bigger share of current world GDP than it was then, so it can be much more consequential. How does it compare to the 2008-2009 recession? That was a different crisis. In 2008-2009, you had a big financial crisis, but that affected banks; it was much deeper and came through the housing sector. It was different. This comparison is more like the dot-com era because it's all about tech and what could happen if you have a tech boom that ultimately doesn't deliver on its promise, especially in terms of profitability. So, I still think we're in a space where valuations are stretched. It is difficult for me to see how, in this hyper-competitive environment, all these companies could make the kinds of profits that justify their level of valuations we're seeing now. So, we could see corrections. That's not a statement about the technology; I think the technology is going to be great. But whether all these companies are going to make the size of profits that justify the valuations, I still have questions on that. So, I think that is a very important risk to keep in mind because financial conditions are actually quite easy everywhere in the world, though that keeps changing on a daily basis depending on news coming in. But that is a shoe that did not drop. We did not have a financial crisis despite five years of pandemic, Russia invading Ukraine, and interest rates going up very sharply. Could we be building up to a financial crisis? I am concerned about that. I bet people ask you every day: if there's a bubble, when is it going to burst? How do you answer that? There is no formula for predicting exactly when a bubble will burst; it could take some time. Usually, there's a trigger. One of the triggers, which is what happened last time right before the dot-com bust, was the Federal Reserve raised interest rates because they were worried about inflation. Could we have a situation this time where the Fed needs to raise interest rates because of tariffs, additional tariffs being put on that get passed through into consumer prices, because of the otherwise general high levels of demand in the US that we're seeing? So if there is surprise inflation and the Fed has to raise interest rates, which is not something markets are expecting, then you could see a sudden tightening in financial conditions, and that could prick a bubble. Let's talk about the Fed then. There is some pressure on it from the political side, from President Trump. How important is it? Is it an orthodox view that central banks should be independent of the political cycle so that they can set long-term policies that can contain inflation? What do you see? Is that a risk that independence might be eroded in the US and potentially elsewhere? I'd say there are two kinds of policies on which economists agree. One is that trade helps countries and people; yes, it may have some flaws and needs to be carefully managed, but in general, it is a positive. The second is central bank independence, which means that if you want to have low inflation in your country, which then will keep interest rates low, you need to have an independent central bank. To be clear, when we say independent central bank, that does not mean the central bank is not accountable to anybody. This is about operational independence, meaning that given the mandate that has come from the political class, from Congress in the case of the US, the actual execution of that mandate stays within an independent central bank. Let's go back to these risks to growth. You mentioned the potential of a tech bubble, or something like the dot-com bubble a couple of decades ago. There are geopolitical risks, aren't there? We've already mentioned Greenland, Iran, Venezuela, all places that America is potentially involved in. There are always geopolitical risks, but do you think we're at a particularly risky moment now? The geopolitical risks we're seeing right now are profound for at least our generation, if not for at least two generations. We had 80 years of a peace dividend, especially after 1990 and the end of the Cold War. Countries were coming together, with closer integration, peace, and growth. It was not perfect; it was absolutely the case that jobs were lost in some countries because of too much open trade. That was a risk that I think we all have to deal with now. But that said, what we're seeing now, especially with this shift in the relationship between the US and Europe, is something I don't think anybody predicted last year in Davos. There was this sense that we could see some decoupling between the West and China, but the fact that there would be a breaking up of the alliance of the West is not something we predicted. Does that have global implications, or is it just Europe needing to sort itself out? Are China, Russia, and the Global South looking at that relationship between the USA and Europe? It has huge global implications. If you look at Europe itself, Europe is now going and making trade relations with other countries, like the deal on work they signed recently. They've been working on it for many years, and though there's still been pushback from member states, they pushed it through. I think it's a statement that Europe believes it needs to have other friends and build those other relationships. Similarly, in their dealings with China, the global order is being shifted around who you work with; new alliances are being formed, however temporary they may be, but those shifts are happening. This is a very consequential relation between the US and Europe, and ruptures of the kind we're seeing will have big implications for everyone. Another risk that economists always flag up is debt, national debt, government debt being a big risk. Is that just being overshadowed? It's still there, right? If we didn't have this large scale of geopolitical tension and tariffs, that's the risk we would be talking about, which is debt. The fact that almost all countries in the world are sitting on record high levels of debt without a clear path on how that will be brought down is indeed a very important risk we are facing in addition to everything else. So, where can growth come from? You pointed out over the last year massive growth stimulated by investment in AI, which had knock-on effects around the world. You're worried, to whatever extent, that this might be a bubble. Are there other areas where we could achieve economic growth? I do think AI will help with economic growth. There are going to be productivity effects, whether it takes a couple of years or longer, we have to see. But I think that is an important source of growth because it's a general-purpose technology; it can transform all fields we're looking at. Of course, what we do need to keep our eye on is what's happening with jobs. Are we going to be creating growth that is jobless? That is a risk we need to worry about, and I am concerned that especially if this transformation happens very rapidly, there's a real risk of that happening. You could end up with a shrinking of the middle class, leading to civil conflict, people protesting. People will not stay quiet in the face of lots of people losing their jobs. How long do you think that will take? At this moment, given what we know, I think it's quite uncertain, the effect on productivity. We still have a fairly wide range of estimates, anywhere between adding 0.1% to productivity growth on a yearly basis to 1.5% of productivity growth. So, there is a big range there. Similarly, regarding the impact on jobs, there is a concern that young people coming on the job market are having a harder time finding jobs because of AI's ability to replace them. But at the same time, there are also young people who are going much more quickly into entrepreneurship because the technology helps them start new enterprises. So, we're still in the phase of figuring this out. The energy price and the oil price are very important when it comes to how the economy functions. I wonder whether, again, it's like you said, "everything changed, nothing changed." You've had the intervention in Venezuela, concerns over what might happen in Iran—these countries with massive fossil fuel reserves—but the oil price, I don't think, has changed that much compared to how dramatic the geopolitical situation is. Where do you see oil prices and energy prices in general going? Energy prices, oil especially, are going to be in the low 60s. That's the most likely outcome. The reason why Venezuela didn't have any big effect is because even though it has a large amount of supply, getting that out of the ground is going to take time. There's been such damage to their infrastructure that this is not going to happen overnight. Iran is about 1% of the oil market, so it is consequential, but it is not a huge effect on oil markets as such, and there's a lot of supply coming from other parts of the world. What about the energy transition? That was often touted, not least here in Davos, as a driver of growth: investment in new types of energy or different types of energy, green energy, energy storage. We're hearing less about that in the headlines right now. Is there still an energy transition going on, and is it somewhere that people should be investing in and looking for growth? There is an energy transition going on, and for several countries, especially emerging and developing countries that otherwise would import a lot of their energy, that is a source of growth for them. It's not just about sustainability but also about job creation and having to rely less on imported fuel. This is going to be an important source of growth for them. They're investing in solar, wind, hydro, all those different forms of sustainable energy. That's a direction in which that will continue. China is doing a lot; I think China is continuing to be on the forefront of the green transition. India is also paying attention, doing the investments that are needed. Pollution is a big challenge in India right now, so even if you are not so worried about sustainability from a global perspective, the fact that you have to get rid of the high levels of pollution itself will move you in the green direction, and weather events are highly consequential for the world, and that will remain the case. You mentioned China. I wonder if you could give us an overview of where you see China's economy going, because it's a very different type of economy to the places we've mostly been talking about until now, North America and Europe. China has relied quite a bit on export-led growth in these last two years because if you look at domestic consumption, that has been fairly weak, and they haven't solved that problem. In China, growth for 2025 is around 5%; it's projected to go down to 4.5%. They still have a problem with their property sector. There was overinvestment in that particular sector, and we still haven't seen reallocation of resources away from property to other markets efficiently. That is still a challenge. It's an aging population; the population shrank for the fourth year in a row—that's another headwind. Productivity growth in China has not been very strong. For all those reasons, if you look out into the medium term, we should expect China's growth to slow to around 3.5% or so. China cannot rely on export-driven growth for multiple reasons. One, it's just too large an economy to be able to grow just on the export side. Secondly, other countries are not going to take in such large amounts of goods; they will slap tariffs very quickly, and they would be justified to do that, even by WTO rules, because of anti-dumping reasons. So, China does need to move towards a growth strategy that is more domestic-demand driven. That's going to require stronger social safety nets for households in China so that people don't feel they have to save that much. And they have to invest in productivity, moving away from state-owned capital towards much more private enterprise. They've made a start in that. Do you see that continuing? They had made a big shift in that right after the great financial crisis, but then after the pandemic, they seem to have drifted sideways again, back into export-led growth. The most recent 15th plan they came up with still talks about investing in new sectors—frontier sectors like AI and frontier energy sectors, and so on—and not enough on the need to have high levels of consumption. So, I'm not sure they've made the switch. I think maybe silently, quietly, they recognize that they cannot have overproduction in every sector. That is a problem because you're going to end up worrying about prices going too low, which is a problem that China has and no other country does. So, they need to do more in terms of reorienting the economy inwards, relying more on consumption and local demand-driven growth as opposed to external growth. I don't see a big dramatic shift in the policies being made, so this is going to take time. I suspect they'll only move slowly. Industrial policy. This used to be seen as a thing of the past, certainly in the West, with governments getting involved in economies that many economists would say, 'let the market sort this out.' It's come back, hasn't it? Where do you see industrial policy going from here? Industrial policy has been around for a while, and I would say China did a lot of it for many years. It's not just in the form of providing a direct subsidy or a tax break to companies, but also in terms of cheap credit and cheap land that generated very large amounts of production capacity in many different sectors, including automobiles, electric vehicles, and also solar, and so on in the case of China. Now, other countries are also reacting partly to what happened in China, but also now there is a big concern, especially after Russia's invasion of Ukraine, about national security concerns. So, there is this need to encourage certain kinds of sectors at home, be it chips, rare earths, energy, or defense, to ensure that some level of national security is still maintained. So, I expect industrial policy is going to be here for a while. It is still important, though, to have guardrails because you could end up in a situation where governments are spending a whole lot of money which they just cannot afford given their level of debt. So, if you're going to do it, do it in a targeted manner on a temporary basis; that's going to be very important. What are the risks of it, though, if it's done badly? If it's done badly, firstly, you're not going to get any of the benefits of the industrial policy because you end up misallocating resources, and you don't see that in growth, and you don't see enough jobs being created. That would be one consequence. The second, of course, is if you end up spending a whole lot and the economy doesn't grow, then your debt as a ratio of GDP is going to go up even more, and how are you going to pay your bills? So, interest rates will go up, and that will just be bad for all other parts of the economy that are not benefiting from industrial policy. So we're sitting here in Davos, looking out to the rest of 2026. I'm going to ask you what you expect to happen in 2026. I'll just quote again from your article in the Financial Times: "The reality is that 2025 was a year when everything changed. The question now is whether 2026 will be the year we correct course." Extended interview: Tom Llamas exclusive with President Trump

NBC News(11.7M) ·50:17 · 2026-02-05
Donald Trump Amazon Keynote
President Trump discussed his administration's strong stance on immigration enforcement, claiming success in lowering crime rates nationwide and on the border, while also outlining his economic policies including tariffs and potential rebate checks, ...
TL;DR President Trump discussed his administration's strong stance on immigration enforcement, claiming success in lowering crime rates nationwide and on the border, while also outlining his economic policies including tariffs and potential rebate checks, and his views on AI's...

TL;DR

President Trump discussed his administration's strong stance on immigration enforcement, claiming success in lowering crime rates nationwide and on the border, while also outlining his economic policies including tariffs and potential rebate checks, and his views on AI's impact, election integrity, and global relations.

Immigration, Crime, and Law Enforcement Strategy

  • Minneapolis ICE Officer Reduction:
      • Action: 700 federal officers were pulled from Minneapolis by Tom Homan, a decision attributed to President Trump.
      • Reasoning: Stated that the withdrawal was due to local authorities not releasing "murderers" and "bad people" that his administration aimed to deport.
      • Goal: To push cities to cooperate in handing over dangerous criminals.
  • National Crime and Enforcement Claims:
      • Crime Reduction: Claims crime is down in Minneapolis, Chicago (by 25%), and other major cities due to his administration's efforts.
      • Law Enforcement Backing: Emphasizes unwavering support for law enforcement (ICE, police), stating, "If we don't back them, we don't have a country."
      • Targeted Deportations: Current mission focuses on deporting "criminals, really bad criminals," including 11,888 alleged murderers, drug dealers, and mentally ill individuals "emptied into our country" from places like Venezuela and Congo.
      • Local Cooperation: Prefers to be "invited" by cities (e.g., Chicago, Philadelphia, San Francisco, Louisiana) to intervene in crime rather than forcing federal intervention.
      • Data Claim: Asserts the U.S. has "record low crime... for 125 years" and the "safest country since 1900."
      • Officer Conduct: Acknowledged "mistakes" in the Renee Good and Alex Pretti incidents in Minnesota, calling both "very sad," but defended ICE officers as "strong, tough people" dealing with hardened criminals.
  • Border Security:
      • Success Metrics: Credits policies for "historic lows" in illegal border crossings and a "beautifully closed" border.
      • Caravans: Stated that countries sent "people that they didn't want" in caravans, including criminals and those unwilling to work.
      • Kristi Noem: Defends her role, attributing the closed border to her efforts, but noted "public relations" challenges in getting credit.
  • Public Perception:
      • Poll Discrepancy: Dismisses polls showing low approval for his immigration handling, stating they are "almost dishonest" and depend on how questions are asked.
      • "Public Relations" Issue: Believes his administration does a "phenomenal job" but is "not good at public relations," failing to "sell the great job that we're doing."

Economic Outlook and Policy

  • Investment and Growth:
      • Investment Claim: States "$18 trillion being invested into the country," contrasting it with "less than a trillion" under the previous administration.
      • Outlook: Expresses confidence in economic growth, stating that growth "will soon make the debt look very small."
  • Inflation Control:
      • Inherited Situation: Claims to have "inherited the worst inflation in the history of our country" (48 years).
      • Current Status: Asserts "almost no inflation" now, citing 1.2% for the last three months.
      • Gasoline Prices: Points to recent gasoline prices at $1.99 a gallon, down from $4.50-$5.
  • Tariffs and Rebates:
      • Tariff Revenue: States that China is paying "hundreds of billions of dollars in tariffs," with "trillions" generated by the threat of tariffs, making the U.S. rich.
      • Farmer Support: Used tariff revenue to provide $12 billion to farmers.
      • Rebate Checks: Seriously considering $2,000 rebate checks for Americans from tariff revenue, confirming he "can do that" and may make the commitment.
      • Military Payment: Mentioned giving $1,776 to the military as part of his budget.
  • Federal Reserve and Interest Rates:
      • Future Rates: Confident that interest rates "are going to be lowered" and "should" be, stating current rates are "way high."
      • Fed Chair Selection: Implied his new Fed pick understands the desire for lower rates, noting that a nominee wanting to raise rates "would not have gotten the job."
      • Independence vs. Influence: While acknowledging the Fed is "in theory... an independent body," he states he knows "the economy better than almost everybody" and implies his influence is expected.
  • Government Spending and Employment:
      • Federal Workers: Claims to have "gotten rid of hundreds of thousands of federal workers," contrasting this with Democrats who "hire 2 million people."
      • Employment Claim: States "more jobs right now occupied... than at any time during its existence, 250 years."

Domestic Politics and Election Integrity

  • Election Corruption Allegations:
      • Local Corruption: Did not use "nationalized," but asserts "extremely corrupt" elections in specific areas like Detroit, Philadelphia, and Atlanta.
      • Voter ID: Argues Democrats oppose voter ID because "they want to cheat on elections," despite claiming "Democrat voters want it."
      • Federal Controls: Advocates for federal controls over elections if states fail to ensure honesty, proper, and timely processes.
  • Midterm Elections:
      • Trust in Results: Will trust midterm results "if the elections are honest."
      • Past Election Claims: Reiterates belief of "cheating" in past elections, despite claiming to have won "all seven swing states" and 84% of counties.
  • Fulton County FBI Investigation:
      • Warrant: Notes an FBI investigation in Fulton County, Georgia, regarding election ballots, operating under a court order signed by a "liberal judge."
      • Purpose: Believes the investigation will "find out the true winner of that state" and expose past "cheating."
  • Foreign Election Interference:
      • China: Believes China attempts to influence U.S. elections.
      • Russia: Dismisses claims of Russian interference as a "hoax," attributing it to Hunter Biden.
  • Legal Battles and Political Targeting:
      • IRS Lawsuit: Suing the IRS for $10.5 billion over leaked tax documents, pledging to donate any winnings to charity.
      • Mar-a-Lago Raid: Claims to have won a lawsuit regarding the "illegal" FBI raid on Mar-a-Lago, calling it a break-in by "very smart, radical left people."
      • Impeachments and Indictments: Highlights having been impeached twice ("fake impeachments") and indicted "87 different times," equating to "287 years" in prison if he had lost.
      • DOJ's Role: While he is the "Chief Law Enforcement Officer," he states he doesn't "choose to" be involved in prosecuting political enemies, but suggests that the intense scrutiny he faced justifies such actions by others.
      • Bill Clinton & Epstein: Expresses a surprising positive sentiment towards Bill Clinton and called the attempts to subpoena him regarding Epstein a "shame," noting Democrats might retaliate by targeting him if Clinton is pursued.

AI and Foreign Policy Engagements

  • Artificial Intelligence (AI):
      • Impact: Considers AI "a big deal," potentially "bigger than the Internet," with vast implications.
      • U.S. Leadership: Claims the U.S. is "leading China by a lot" and "leading everybody by a lot" in AI development.
      • Economic Opportunity: Predicts AI will be the "greatest jobs producer," "greatest military producer," and "greatest medical producer," dismissing concerns about job losses.
      • Energy Infrastructure: Proposes that new AI and auto plants will build their "own electric plant" to meet demand, as the country will need "twice the electric."
  • China Relations:
      • Positive Relationship: Describes a "very good relationship" with President Xi, with plans for reciprocal visits (Trump to China in April, Xi to White House late year).
      • Discussions: Confirms discussions on the economy and tariffs.
  • Iran Policy:
      • Threat Assessment: Warns Iran's Supreme Leader "should be very worried" due to U.S. actions.
      • Nuclear Program: Claims his administration "wiped out their nuclear" program, leading to "peace in the Middle East."
      • Military Action: States B-2 bombers "obliterated" targets, preventing a nuclear weapon within a month. Threatens renewed force if Iran attempts to restart its nuclear program at new sites.
  • Cuba Situation:
      • Crisis: States Cuba is "in big trouble" and needs "sanitarian aid," citing the cessation of financial support from Venezuela.
      • Engagement: Confirms the U.S. is "talking to Cuba" to explore options for Cuban-Americans to visit their loved ones.
      • Political Support: Notes strong support from Cuban-American voters (94%).

Presidential Legacy and Personal Reflections

  • White House Projects:
      • Oval Office Decor: Enhanced Oval Office with historical presidential portraits and the Declaration of Independence.
      • Building for Legacy: Overseeing construction of a new White House Ballroom, a "Triumphal Arc" taller than Paris' Arc de Triomphe, and a "Garden of Heroes."
      • Purpose: Aims to bring "glamor, prestige, beauty, and strength" back to the country and cement his legacy.
      • Security Features: The new ballroom will feature "drone-proof ceilings" (drones bounce off) and "bulletproof windows" reflecting modern security concerns.
  • Personal Safety and Health:
      • Safety Concerns: Expresses worry about his personal safety as President, calling it "the most dangerous job there is," statistically more hazardous than race car driving or bronco riding.
      • Mental and Physical Health: States he feels "great, physically and mentally," like he did "50 years ago."
      • Cognitive Testing: Regularly takes and "aced" cognitive physicals, noting no other President has agreed to do them, and criticizes former President Biden's perceived cognitive decline.
      • Medication: Continues to take Aspirin as he has for 30 years, preferring to keep his blood "nice and thin."
      • Self-Awareness: Believes he would know if his age began to impact his ability to perform his job.

Notable Quotes

  • "We have record low crime in the United States. Nobody has been able to say that for 125 years." – *President Trump*
  • "I don't believe the polls... The polls are almost dishonest, almost as dishonest as some of the reporters themselves." – *President Trump*
  • "AI is a big deal. AI is going to be maybe the biggest thing. Bigger than the Internet, bigger than anything else." – *President Trump*
  • "Being President is the most dangerous job there is." – *President Trump*
