OpenAI CEO Sam Altman discussed the launch of GPT-5.3-Codex, highlighting its role in enabling a future where users manage AI agents at higher levels of abstraction for complex tasks. He emphasized the growing importance of compute power as the "new oil" and the fundamental transformation of software, while also outlining OpenAI's advertising strategy focused on reducing AI's "capability overhang."

New Models & Agent-Driven Workflows

  • GPT-5.3-Codex Launch: OpenAI has launched GPT-5.3-Codex, described as the world's best and fastest coding model with improved personality and computer usage capabilities.
      • Mid-Turn Interaction: A key feature is the ability to interact with the model mid-turn, enabling users to steer and correct agents during long, multi-hour tasks for superior outcomes.
  • Agent Orchestration: Future workflows will involve users managing "teams of agents" and operating at increasingly higher levels of abstraction as agent capabilities advance.
      • Capability Overhang: There is a significant "intelligence overhang," making the development of better management tools for agents more critical than raw intelligence increases for the immediate future.
      • Codex Desktop: The Codex desktop app has seen surprising user adoption, demonstrating how small improvements in user experience ("10% of polish") can dramatically increase the utility of powerful models.
          • General-Purpose Agent: The vision extends to a general knowledge work agent that can control computers and handle complex tasks for non-technical users (e.g., an auto-completing to-do list).
      • Mobile Integration: Future plans include mobile capabilities, allowing users to initiate tasks and interact with their unified AI backend across various devices.
      • OpenClaw Spirit: Altman praised the "OpenClaw spirit" of individual open-source innovation, which can often develop features faster than large companies constrained by legal and privacy considerations.

Strategic Resources & Industry Shifts

  • Compute as the New Oil: While "data is the new oil" was an overstatement a decade ago, "compute power is the new oil" is now the closest accurate description, reflecting the exponential increase in resources driving AI model improvements.
      • Bottlenecks: Currently, chips are the primary bottleneck, and society needs to aggressively increase global wafer capacity.
  • Revival of Research Labs: Altman noted a positive trend in the resurgence of industry-backed research labs, a goal OpenAI initially aimed to inspire.
      • Acquisition Strategy: Future acquisitions will likely be hybrid entities that blend extraordinary product work with a strong research component.
  • Software Evolution: Software is not dead but undergoing a significant transformation, with expected volatility in the SaaS market.
      • API-First World: "Every company is an API company now whether they want to be or not," as agents will increasingly interact directly with various services.
      • Consumer-Centric Integration: Companies like Uber are recognizing the necessity of integrating with preferred AI agents, prioritizing consumer experience over platform lock-in.
  • Forward-Deployed Engineers: These engineers are crucial for integrating AI into non-AI-native companies, addressing challenges such as system integration, fine-tuning, orchestration, and critical data security.

AI's Impact & Future Outlook

  • Shifting Benchmarks: Traditional AI benchmarks are rapidly becoming obsolete; "GDP impact" may soon be the most relevant metric, with "happiness" potentially following.
  • Human-AI Relationships: Altman's 2016 prediction of emotional attachment to chatbots is now a tangible reality and a significant concern.
      • Deflationary Impact: AI could lead to scenarios where the quality of life dramatically improves, even if traditional GDP measures decline, posing a new challenge for societal measurement.
  • Model IQ & Scale: Altman anticipates models will become vastly more intelligent, capable of processing information for durations equivalent to "10,000 human years," though the societal impact might feel "very weird" rather than "impossibly weird" due to a human-centric focus.
  • Social AI: While current AI bots on existing social platforms are problematic, Altman is interested in designing new social experiences where agents interact on behalf of people, focusing on "assertion of humanity" over AI detection.
  • Sora & Creative Use Cases: Sora's video generation is primarily used for personal, niche content like memes and customized character images (e.g., caricatures or enhanced self-portraits), rather than mass consumption of AI-generated videos.
      • Tool Use: Models will become highly capable at utilizing motion graphics and editing tools like After Effects.
  • Internal Focus Amidst Scrutiny: Despite intense media scrutiny and public anxieties, Altman describes OpenAI's internal environment as "less chaotic" and highly focused on building new models and addressing challenges like the compute crunch.

Advertising & Competitive Landscape

  • OpenAI's Advertising Strategy: OpenAI's first Super Bowl ad was not a direct-response campaign but aimed at researchers and those driving the AI revolution.
      • New Ad Goal: Future advertising, including an upcoming ad for Codex, will focus on teaching people "what they can go do with AI" to reduce the general "capability overhang."
      • Ad Rollout: OpenAI has yet to begin testing ads and expects an iterative process to determine the most effective ad units and approach, acknowledging its rapid transition from a research lab to a large company.
  • Anthropic's "Attack Ads": Altman characterized Anthropic's Super Bowl ads as "wrong" and "deceptive," stating that OpenAI respects its users and would not integrate ads into the LLM stream in a "dystopic" manner.
      • "Playing Dirty": He found the ads ironic, using deception to criticize deceptive ads, and called the competitive exchange a "sideshow" compared to the models' actual capabilities.

Notable Quotes

"I think we will be heading towards a workflow where a lot of people just feel like they're managing a team of agents and they'll keep as the agents get better, they'll keep operating at a higher and higher level of abstraction." — Sam Altman, CEO, OpenAI "On the whole, compute power is the new oil is the statement that feels closest to true to me, but there will be other parts too." — Sam Altman, CEO, OpenAI "The statement that someone said to me that has stuck in my mind most these last couple of weeks is that every company is an API company now whether they want to be or not." — Sam Altman, CEO, OpenAI "I think AI is now unbelievably capable in most of the world. It's still like asking it basic questions on Chad GBT... Everyone can go build amazing things now. Everyone can go do all kinds of work." — Sam Altman, CEO, OpenAI
Welcome to the show, Sam. How are you doing? Good. Thank you for having me back. Big day. Kick us off with where should we start? Should we start with the model or Frontier? Can we start with the model just because I'm having fun with it? Absolutely. Break it down. What did you launch? We launched 5.3 Codex. It is, I think, the best coding model in the world. We took a lot of the feedback that people had about 5.2 Codex and got it into one model. It is much smarter at programming, but it's also way faster. You can interact with it mid-turn. I think it has a much better personality. It's really good at computer use. It feels like a very big step forward. It was funny as we were deploying it this morning, a couple of very, extremely experts at using these models noticed and said, "Man, something's really different with Codex." They caught it mid-deploy. So, I think you can really feel it quickly. That's great. You're saying people outside of OpenAI, just everyday users, noticed it immediately. Tell us about interacting with it mid-turn. How does that work? Why is that important? What does that unlock? People are starting to use these tools for very long pieces of work at one time, multi-hour tasks. Sometimes you don't specify it correctly, or something's not set up right, something just screws up. They can do amazing things with no steering, but they can do much more amazing things if you steer them along the way. This has been one of the things I felt was most new about this model. Talk about orchestration, how this fits into Frontier, because it's notable. If you see a coworker making a mistake and you don't interrupt them, that's rude; it's deeply inefficient. It is incredible what these models can do without any feedback. If you think about a new coworker especially, you train them and give them a lot of feedback early on, and they learn the job. You correct them, and they get practice. The models will soon do that, but right now they don't. So we just rely on either they get it right one shot or we correct them along the way. I think there are a lot of people running multiple agents and multiple tabs. They're starting to think about orchestration. Frontier feels like a piece of that, but if you're interacting with a model that's running mid-turn, how does the user experience change for developers with 5.3, and then what will it look like in the Frontier world? I think we will be heading towards a workflow where a lot of people just feel like they're managing a team of agents. As the agents get better, they'll keep operating at a higher and higher level of abstraction, which, at least watching what's happening so far, is a jump that people are going to make pretty well. The models are so good now; there's such a capability overhang that building better tools to let people do that—which the Codex app we launched on Monday was a great step forward for—will be very, very important. You will be managing very complex workflows. The agents will keep getting better, so you'll keep working at the maximum of your management bandwidth or cognitive ability to keep track of all the stuff. The tools to make that easy to do will matter more than intelligence for a little while because there's such an intelligence overhang already. What's the role of a forward-deployed engineer today at OpenAI towards the end of the year? Capability overhang feels like raw meat for a forward-deployed engineer. They solve that problem. Eventually, the models will get so good that they'll help companies deploy themselves, and the forward-deployed engineers will again get to work at a higher level of abstraction. But for now, you go into a company that is not AI native, and they say they want to deploy AI, but they're not sure what to do. How do I hook this up to my systems? Do I need to fine-tune a model on my codebase? How do I think about orchestrating agents and using things from different companies? Most of all, or at least what we hear most frequently, is how do I think about security of my data and how do I know that these AI co-working agents are not going to access a bunch of information and share it in ways they shouldn't, or get a context exploit or something like that? So the forward engineers take this incredible new technology and a platform like Frontier and say, "We will connect your company to an AI platform so that you can use all these agents and workflows and everything else you want." How important are these metaphors, or how temporary are they? I was very interested in reading about Gas Town, and you have these pole cats, and it's this whole Mad Max world. That feels like maybe just a temporary aberration where you're setting up agents for specific tasks, but also that could be incredibly valuable in explaining to a large corporation how they're going to integrate AI across the whole organization. I suspect, like everything else that's happening when an industry is moving so fast, all of this is somewhat temporary on a long enough time scale. As these models become more capable and these agents are operating on very long time horizons with the ability to just figure it out, and our trust in their robustness keeps going up, then maybe you don't need a lot of the abstractions we need today. Maybe you just have a single AI bot that runs at your company, and you can say, "Hey, I want to launch this new product," and it does everything an ambitious person would do. But that's not where we are today. So today we have to put in a little more work to get the pieces put together. How are you thinking about the meter benchmark for long task horizons? You're at the top of the charts. At the same time, it feels like we might need a new chart if we're talking about agent swarms because they'll be able to do things that go for weeks, but they will subdivide the work. There's some subdivision that happens within a reasoning model, but it doesn't truly parallelize, at least that I'm aware of. What does it look like in a world where you go to a model, but now it's spinning up a whole bunch of different models underneath? There are two key insights of the whole field in this question. Number one, the implication that no chart in AI lasts more than a few years is right. We'll see how much longer this one is useful. The second is that a lot of people thought that we were just going to need a super long task and a super long task horizon, so we needed a super long context. What people have already seen with coding agents is that by agents breaking up work, orchestrating it well, farming it off to sub-agents, even with the current limitations of the technology, we can do something which should not be surprising because it's similar to how people do things and get amazing amounts of work done. That's been cool to watch, and I think that will keep going. A joke that some people at OpenAI make is that soon the chart that matters is just going to be GDP impact. And then the question is what comes after that? All these other proxy metrics, there's now so much economic value in what the models are doing. What do you think could come after? I have no idea. Do you have an opinion? Happiness. When you look back at some of your blog posts from 10 years ago, your predictions were usually pretty on point. Thank you. Specifically, the merge, basically. There are a bunch that are good. Right now, we're getting thousands of messages in the chat about 40, and you predicted in 2016 that people would become very attached to a chatbot. I'm working on a big prediction blog post for the next five years. It seems too far for 10 years, but it's still fun to try. The relationships with chatbots are clearly something now we have to worry about more and are no longer an abstract concept. Even the question of what comes after GDP is interesting because the way we measure GDP now could start going down even though quality of life goes way up. We don't have a lot of practice with things like that, or with the massively deflationary aspect where quality of life goes up even as we measure GDP going down. Switching gears, what do you think about the work that the Neol boom, the research efforts that are happening all over Silicon Valley, signifies? It feels like there's an acknowledgment that there are research breakthroughs that need to happen, and everyone's taking different shots at those. Do you think that those companies will just find a breakthrough and join a lab or launch their own products? I think it's fantastic. One of the meta things we wanted to do when we started OpenAI is that there had been a period where the technology industry, and Silicon Valley in particular, was amazing at new research labs or doing new research in industry in general. Then it fell apart, and there hadn't been a good one in a while. Part of what we hoped to show—and this was not only us, a lot of people were excited about new research labs—is that industry could do research again. So seeing that now become fashionable in all of these new labs, I think it's totally awesome. Some will succeed, some will fail, some will go into some other effort, but having industry support research in startup style, I think it's wonderful. Over the next two years, would you expect to acquire more individual product companies or more research labs? Good question. I don't have a strong opinion there. I would bet the very best ones will often look like a mixture of both. The one I have in mind right now very much looks like a mixture of both. So maybe the shape of things to come is that truly extraordinary product work will have more and more of a research component, and it'll be more of a hybrid thing. Is data the new oil? Is there value? We were joking a couple of days ago: "They have a bunch of data, but they don't understand AI. They don't know how to monetize." That phrase was effectively wasted a decade ago, and to say it now sounds really silly, but it feels like it could be more true now than ever. Certainly. They really did waste it a decade ago. I was just thinking of the kind of people that used to say that. They're nice people over at TED. The magic relationship of this last eight years, whatever you want to call it, has been that we can put in more and more resources—compute, data, new ideas, whatever—into creating an artifact, and it gets better. That's why we have this huge exponential increase in resources, but we keep getting better and better models. For all the concern people have about it topping out or slowing down, no one's been right about that. Sometimes it looked like they were for a couple of months as we digested a new model or came to a new form factor, but it has been an incredibly smooth last six or eight years in terms of what those resources are. There can be some trade-off; sometimes it's better to spend your money on better data, sometimes on more compute, sometimes something else. On the whole, "compute power is the new oil" is the statement that feels closest to true to me, but there will be other parts too. Is software dead? It's different. It's definitely not dead. But how you create software, how you're going to use it, how much you're going to have written for you each time you need it versus how much you'll want a consistent UX—that's all going to change. There have been a number of these big sell-offs of SAS stocks over the last few years as these models have rolled out. I expect there will continue to be more. I expect there will be big booms in software. I think it's just going to be volatile for a while as people figure out what this looks like. The statement that someone said to me that has stuck in my mind most these last couple of weeks is that every company is an API company now, whether they want to be or not, because agents are just going to be able to write a CS. We had D from Uber on yesterday, and he had a pretty refreshing approach. We were asking about integrating agents with Uber, and he recognized that the ad business could potentially be threatened if you can order an Uber in ChatGPT, but he basically said you have to think of the consumer. The consumer wants to order an Uber via their preferred agent. You should let them, otherwise you're going to have other problems. That is the right take for sure, and I think so at least. We've been through platform shifts like this before. Uber wouldn't have existed without one. It wasn't until the iPhone that it could make sense to order an Uber right where you were as you're out in the world. I think there will be totally new things that happen and other things you'll use in new ways. But definitely, as I've started using Codex, my excitement about having agents go off and do things for me and still use other services, pay other services, is high. I'm sure we'll need to figure out new business models and how revenue gets shared around, but that will happen. Talk more about desktop. One more question on SAS. Have any public market SAS companies tried to get a soft landing with OpenAI? Do you think there's any value there? No public SAS companies that I'm aware of have tried that with OpenAI. Some of them will certainly be durable and are on sale right now, and potentially just need new energy and an entirely new approach, and maybe OpenAI could provide that. I think some will be incredibly valuable. Some do feel like a thinner layer now. I don't know; I was talking recently to a bunch of SAS companies and they don't feel unexcited. They believe they're going through a big transformation. They say, "Sure, other people can instantly write software now, but so can we, and we have a great system of record." It seems reasonable. Some won't make it, of course. Talk more about the Codex desktop rollout. It feels like there's been a successful amount of downloads, but also a key shift for people who are maybe lightly technical but don't have time to set up an IDE and configure an environment to actually start writing software. I want to know about plans to integrate to the phone. That was a big moment, I think, for a lot of people with the OpenClaw thing; it was, "Oh, I can text something and it will go and write code," and that's valuable, and that unlocks a new agentic experience. Where do you see the Codex desktop ecosystem going? Codex Desktop has been somewhat of a surprise to me in terms of how much people love it, including how much I love it myself. I think it's a great example of how 10% of polish on the experience of using these models, especially when there's so much capability overhang, goes an extremely long way to what you can build and how you interact with this stuff. Of course, we should have an ability to kick off new tasks from mobile, and we'll do that. Really, what you want is your single AI working for you on a unified backend, with access to all of your data, your ideas, your stuff, your memory, all the context, and the ability to work across a lot of surfaces. Often you'll be at your desktop, often you'll be on your phone, and you just want to add something in. But it is a pretty profound shift in my own workflow, not just for coding tasks, but more general-purpose tasks. It's still hard to use if you're not at least reasonably technical. But obviously, we'll find a version of this product that can do other knowledge work tasks and control your computer, where you don't have to be technical. It will bring the magic of building stuff to a lot of people because even if you never look at code, you'll be able to build something reasonably sophisticated. One of the things I built when I was playing around with the new Codex app is this thing I had always wanted: a magic autocompleting to-do list. I really work with to-do lists, and this idea that I could just put tasks in and it would try to do them. If it could complete them, it would complete them. If it needed questions, it would ask me questions. If I had to do something, I could still do it the old-fashioned way. An interface like that, where all the stuff you want to do, you explain to a computer or your AI, and it tries to go off and do them—and sure, if you're on your phone, you're going to just add a task on your phone, or if you want to easily import something from email, you're going to do that—feels really good. So, I'm excited about all the ways that this will just become a general knowledge work agent. Were you unsurprised to see a product like OpenClaw come from open source? That kind of user experience is something that you knew would be a thing, and yet I think part of the magic of OpenClaw is that it would be very, very difficult for a large tech company. Peter didn't make many phone calls to hyperscalers to say, "Hey, I'm going to be integrating your API." It just went. When I think back on the Sky acquisition, this kind of experience was probably very top of mind and things that you're working towards internally. I love the spirit of everything about OpenClaw. You are totally right that it's much easier to imagine a one-person open source project doing something like that than a company that is going to be afraid of lawsuits and data privacy and everything else. I think this is how innovation works. Something like that starts, is clearly amazing. There will be a way to make a mass market version of that product, but letting the builders build, letting the equivalent of the Homebrew Computer Club spirit go here, is so important. Can we switch to social? If I Google "Sam Altman social," I get pure AI and Sora, and then also predictions about a human social network. Where do you see social going broadly? How do you want to integrate with it and power it in the future? The "mold book thing" was a very interesting social experiment to watch, and I think it points to agents interacting in social spaces, hopefully on behalf of people to some degree, which could be quite interesting. I don't think we know what to do there yet, but it feels like social is going to change a lot. I am interested in the space of what a social experience can look like when your agent is talking to my agent and coming up with new stuff. Clearly, putting a lot of AI bots on the existing social platforms is just making everyone crazy and not that fun. So that's not the right answer. I think we can design something new for what this technology is capable of that will feel good and useful. Is there a solution to the bot problem that's just all the labs integrating with all the other platforms? Even if you can't detect it's AI generated, you can literally say, "We just generated those tokens; those exact tokens are in our database." You can't do that because open source models are good enough to write at this point. I am excited about the assertion of humanity instead of the detection of AI as a thing here. I don't know if the social platforms are in their interest to solve this. It is, because in the short term it creates a lot of engagement and increased usage. I believe they could solve it if they wanted to, but I'm not sure it's in their interest. I don't like it, but some people do seem to enjoy it. Can you talk a little bit more about where Sora, as a video generation model, is going? It feels like tool use is maybe under-discussed. Adding reasoning. It's not just the diffusion model. It's giving these models the ability to make linear cuts and overlay motion graphics. When I scroll through Instagram reels, I see vibe reels with cutouts, and they flip negative, and it's all color-graded. It's stuff that, yes, you could probably diffuse it all, but it's pretty cool to teach a model to also use After Effects or whatever video motion graphics suite you want to use. Is that an interesting unlock? What do you see going on? All of that stuff will happen, and I agree with you, the models will get really great at doing that. People love generating videos. I would say we have not yet found a way that people really love watching other people's videos. This is true for a lot of other AI. People love talking to ChatGPT, but it's not that compelling for most people to read other people's generation. But isn't that for all writing and all video? It seems stronger to me in this case than the general case. But maybe you're right. If somebody says, "Hey, I generated a 15-minute video. I'm really excited for you to watch it," and you watch the first 10 seconds and you're not that captured by it, I don't care that it was human-made. You may be right, and this is not a special case. Do you see that in the data with Sora downloads? I've noticed that I'll generate stuff on Sora, download it, and share it to a group chat, and then it's this little inside joke that me and five other people get. We see this in family group chats: "Oh, it's our dog and our kids." But there's not really a broader business application. Does everyone like this? Absolutely. The most common use case is something like that. Memes on group chats are a real killer use case for Sora. How is the Disney rollout going? I was super excited about it. Jordy was extremely bullish on it from a business strategy perspective. When you look at how image models have grown, and various LLMs historically, and now you're going to have an image and video model that can do something that no other LLM can do, at least legally. Is Bob Iger joining OpenAI? I would love it if Bob Iger joined OpenAI. He's looking for a job and free a year. He's a free agent, pick him up. Do some recruiting. That would be great. I think that generating characters in images and videos is going to be very important to people, and they really like that. As we were saying, otherwise, I don't think many people want to watch me and some Star Wars character doing something together, but I might think it's cool. There's a real trend going on right now with ChatGPT where it's, "Make a caricature of me and my job based on everything you know about me." Those kinds of things people actually do like looking at other people's media. It's almost like a face filter or something. There's enough of the human still in there that it's personalized, and it says something about you. A lot of what's gone viral before with image generation is if you can make people look a little bit more attractive or cool than they are in real life without having to ask for that. How are you thinking about the actual rollout? We were debating between opening the floodgates so you can generate any Disney property versus having, for example, "Spider-Man week" where everyone posts Spider-Man, and then "Mickey Mouse week" for another viral moment. I'm not sure what the team is planning there. I know Disney has had some different opinions about how they want to do things, and we'll try to be a good partner there. But I'd be excited to open the floodgates personally. That would be fun. Talk about your first Super Bowl ad. It felt like it was not AI-generated, with lots of motion graphics, black dots coming together. What was the goal with that ad? Who were you trying to speak to? It didn't feel like a direct response, QR code, "download the app" kind of ad. What was the mission? I love that ad. I think that was such a cool one. It was clearly not meant to be a mass market or direct response ad. But speaking to the people at the center of this revolution, trying to celebrate everything that has come before and everything that will come after. We didn't hear a lot about it from average users of ChatGPT, but we heard a lot about it from researchers in the field, and there was a lot of resonance there. It was definitely not generated; it was done the old-fashioned way. A lot of people loved it, a lot of people hated it, and many people in the middle didn't get it, and I felt okay about that. It's a great example. I like our ad for Sunday. It's about Codex, no surprise. Talk about the evolution of the advertising to be more clear about the actual use case, the value. What are you trying to say with your advertising strategy now as it relates to video? The thing I would most like us to say, and I think this is a new challenge given where the models are, is to teach people what they can do with AI. I think AI is now unbelievably capable. Most of the world is still just asking it basic questions on ChatGPT. Everyone can build amazing things now. Everyone can do all kinds of work. Scientists are going to make new discoveries. To the degree that advertising we do can teach people how to use this, I think that would be awesome. So, the KPI is to reduce the capability overhang broadly? I think that should be a general KPI for us, not just for our ads. The products that we build, how we teach people to use those products—that feels very important. Anthropic also has a bunch of ads in the Super Bowl. They seem to run a ton. What do you think they're getting wrong about their characterization of how ads will roll out in chat apps? It's just wrong. The main thing is, we are not stupid. We respect our users. We understand that if we did something like what those ads depict, people would rightfully stop using the product. Our first principle with ads is that we're not going to put stuff into the LLM stream. That would feel crazy dystopian, like a bad sci-fi movie. So the main thing that's wrong with the ads is that using a deceptive ad to criticize deceptive ads doesn't sit right with me. I asked Claude what the definition of playing dirty was, and it said, "Misleading others about your intentions, hiding information, or creating false impressions." I thought it was a little dirty. It was well played for sure, and it was a funny ad. They nailed the stuff about the ChatGPT personality that most annoys me and which we'll fix very soon. So that part was funny. I think it's great for them not to do ads. We have a different shaped business. I did notice that they said they may later revise this decision and will explain why. The blog post did a good job of disarming the pro-ad people and gave themselves an out in the future. Do you think they care? It doesn't matter. I think it's a sideshow. People are excited for a food fight between companies, but the amazing capabilities of these models, the product, the groundswell of excitement around Codex—that feels way more important to me. How do you stop the pausing that happens in voice mode? Do you need new hardware for that or is it a model capability thing? We need a new model. We may need some new hardware too, but mostly we just need a new model. I think we will have a great voice mode by the end of this year. What's the bigger bottleneck? Energy or chips? It goes back and forth. Right now, it's chips again. It will be different at different times. Is there anything society, America, should be doing more aggressively to increase the supply of fabs? It may get solved on its own; normal capitalism may solve it. But I think somehow deciding as a society that we are going to increase the wafer capacity of the world, and we're going to fund that, and we're going to get the whole supply chain and the talented people we need to make that happen would be a very good thing to do. Do you think there's an upper bound on model IQ? The race right now is, you're smart, but you're not smart for days, you're smart for hours. Can you go much farther and get much smarter? It seems certain. Upper bound? I don't know how to think about that question yet. I can't even reason about what 2,000 IQ looks like. I don't know what that means. It's funny you say. I can't reason about what it means to think about a problem for 10,000 human years. That's crazy. But maybe IQ is going to feel even weirder. I don't know. I somehow feel like this isn't going to feel as strange as it sounds, for a bunch of reasons. We're so focused on other people. We're so focused on our own lives. We have such a human-centric nature that, okay, this thing is really smart. It's inventing new science for us. It's running companies for us. It's doing all this stuff. That sounds like it should be impossibly weird, and I think it will just be very weird. Do you think space data centers will provide a meaningful amount of compute for OpenAI in the next two to three years, five years? No. 10 years? I wish Ethan luck. The funny thing about the back and forth about ads is that in our world, the criticism is that you didn't launch ads early enough. Is there a world where you wish you launched earlier? How is the actual rollout going? Are advertisers happy? Do you have a really long roadmap, or do you think you'll be faster at catching up to what's frontier in ads? We haven't even started the test yet. We start the test soon. It's going to take us some number of iterations to figure out the right ad unit, the right way this all works. Do I wish we had started earlier? We have gone from not being a company three years and three months ago. We were a research lab, and now we are a pretty big company with a lot of products. So there are many things I wish we had done faster. I think we were correct on the trade-off here of how we balance things that we need to do. I wish we had launched this very cool enterprise platform this morning. I wish we had done that earlier too, but we had to deal with the monstrous growth of ChatGPT and Codex and all other stuff. Good problems to have. Last question for me: what happened to that internal writing model that you used to write the essay? That feels like something that was really cool, but we never really saw the light of day. We're going to get a lot of that spirit into a future model. There's so much stuff happening. We have to make these hard prioritization decisions. I would love a cool writing model, not as much as I would love a cool coding model. What is possible now for coding, for science—that's the thing I'm most excited about. For accelerating all kinds of research, AI and otherwise, for really accelerating the economy. I think that's the right thing for us to most prioritize in terms of new capabilities. You want a model that can write beautifully if it can also think very clearly and express that very clearly. That's just useful in normal work. That makes a ton of sense. Last question for me. How have conversations been with the broad OpenAI leadership team? You guys are in a position where any single word or sentence you say in any situation can be spun into a headline immediately, and then you have to go on damage control, correcting the narrative. But of course, the original news is often seen more broadly than the correction, and it seems like an interesting challenge. It is a strange way to live. I don't know of any private company that has ever been so in the news and under a microscope. At some level it's frustrating, and we're so squarely in the sights of everybody's anxieties and every competitor trying to take us down. Everyone is wondering what will happen with AI to their business or their own lives, so there's a lot of plasma looking for an instability to collapse on. In some other sense, though, the subjective experience of it is that we are so busy with exciting stuff that it often feels like there's a crazy hurricane turning around us, but when we sit, it's fairly calm. The media or Twitter goes insane about something one day; they're talking about a crazy meltdown. We think, "That is insane!" People talk about it all day and then later find out it's wrong, which seems like a lot of wasted energy. But we're just focused: we have this great new model coming, people are building incredible stuff, companies are transforming, and we're trying to figure out how to get more compute and deal with this compute crunch. We just keep going and we're busy. Then, if we open Twitter, pop up our heads, and look at the news, it's like, "Wow, that is an insane, crazy thing happening," completely divorced from reality or 99% divorced from reality. Someone will correct it, but then we get back to work and people flip out again. OpenAI vs. Anthropic, the AI shift that's rattling software stocks and AI Super bowl commercials

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·29:24 ·1.1K views · 2026-02-05
Sam Altman NVIDIA OpenAI Keynote
The AI landscape is experiencing unprecedented acceleration, disrupting traditional software markets and fueling an intense "war" among major AI labs for enterprise, developer, and consumer dominance through increasingly capable, agentic models that ...
TL;DR The AI landscape is experiencing unprecedented acceleration, disrupting traditional software markets and fueling an intense "war" among major AI labs for enterprise, developer, and consumer dominance through increasingly capable, agentic models that empower even...

TL;DR

The AI landscape is experiencing unprecedented acceleration, disrupting traditional software markets and fueling an intense "war" among major AI labs for enterprise, developer, and consumer dominance through increasingly capable, agentic models that empower even non-technical users to build and deploy sophisticated applications.

The Accelerating AI Landscape & Software Market Disruption

  • Rapid Market Shifts: The software sector is experiencing a "broad sweeping mass exodus" (J.P. Morgan) with a significant sell-off impacting project management, CRM, legal tech, video game developers, and financial data companies.
  • Intense AI Competition:
      • OpenClaw (now Maltbot): A viral, open-source AI agent allowing full computer access and autonomous action, created by a single developer.
      • OpenAI's Codex & Frontier: OpenAI launched a new version of Codex (dubbed "most loved internal product") and "Frontier," an enterprise platform for building and managing AI agents capable of "real work across your entire business."
      • Anthropic's Opus 4.6: Anthropic released its "most powerful model yet," Opus 4.6, capable of working in PowerPoint, pulling regulatory filings, and running parallel agents.
      • Direct Confrontation: Anthropic's Super Bowl ad took direct shots at OpenAI (e.g., ads in ChatGPT), prompting a public retort from OpenAI's Sam Altman.
      • Fading Buzz: Earlier models like Gemini 3, which once held significant buzz, are now receiving less attention amidst the rapid release cycle.
  • "War for the Enterprise, Developer, and Consumer": The market has shifted from ChatGPT's uncontested dominance to a full-scale battle for users across all segments.

The "Free" Software Paradigm & Production-Grade AI

  • Explosive Adoption & "Vibe Coding":
      • Massive Deployment Spike: Guillermo Rauch, CEO of Vercel, observed a "14 times larger growth from November to December" in new application deployments, attributing it to widespread "vibe coding" during the holidays.
      • Software is Effectively Free: Rauch posits that all traditional software (bought, installed, configured) is now being automated and generated by AI models, contributing to the broader software stock sell-off.
  • Democratization of Software Creation:
      • From Prototype to Production: Initially, AI-generated code was seen as suitable for prototypes, but models like Anthropic's Opus 4.5 and 4.6 enable "production grade applications."
      • Accessibility for Non-Developers: Even individuals with "zero technical background" can now build and deploy functional applications within hours.
      • Robust Infrastructure: Vibe-coded applications deployed on Vercel utilize the same global cloud infrastructure, security layers, and frameworks (e.g., Next.js) as the internet's largest properties, ensuring scalability and safety.
  • Downward Pressure on Legacy Software:
      • Just-In-Time & Personal Software: Expect an increase in "just in time software," "personal software," and "throwaway software," as the UI layer can be "fully generative."
      • Cost Reduction: This new paradigm implies "downward pricing pressure" on static, expensive, configured legacy software that previously required "month-long procurement cycles," now creatable with "basically two prompts."

The Rise of Agentic AI & Tool-Using Models

  • Agents Gaining Superpowers: AI models gain significant capabilities when they can use tools, allowing them to iterate and debug code like human engineers.
  • Specialized Strengths:
      • Anthropic's Agentic Prowess: Anthropic models excel at "agentic loops" and automating the "operating system," allowing agents to use computers and tools much like humans to achieve desired outputs.
      • OpenAI's Coding Expertise: OpenAI's Codex is highlighted as an "extremely powerful coding model," particularly strong in "expert software engineering tasks."
  • Autonomous Computing & User Experience:
      • "Jarvis Assistant" Analogy: Technologies like OpenClaw (Maltbot) act as a personal Jarvis assistant, automating computing by interfacing via text messages (e.g., WhatsApp, Telegram) for tasks like email management.
      • User-Friendly Computing: This approach is described as "the most user-friendly computing device in history" because users simply "talk to it."
  • Secure Deployment of Agents: Vercel offers "sandboxes" to safely provide agents with freedom and tools (e.g., access to email, calendar), incorporating firewall and virtualization technologies to mitigate risks and enable large-scale cloud deployments of "millions or trillions of these digital workers."
  • Open-Source Model Emergence:
      • Chinese Models Gaining Traction: Due to rate limits and price-performance issues with major AI labs, open-source models (e.g., China's Moonshots Kimmy) are increasingly attractive, offering cheaper and more efficient alternatives, even gaining significant token usage.
      • Vercel's AI Gateway: Facilitates secure access to a diverse portfolio of models, including those from Anthropic, Nvidia, OpenAI, Gemini, and vetted Chinese providers, allowing developers fluidity between different AI stacks.

Competitive Dynamics and Future Outlook

  • Multi-Model Ecosystem: The most capable AI engineers use a "combination of models and tools," leveraging the strengths of each. Vercel's AI Gateway aims to route tasks automatically to the most appropriate and cost-effective model (e.g., cheapest for greetings, frontier for complex math).
  • Caution on Benchmarks: While benchmarks exist, Guillermo Rauch advises caution, stating many are "gamified" and do not reflect real-world effectiveness or customer satisfaction. "Stickiness, retention, and NPS" are the ultimate measures.
  • The Cost-Benefit Analysis: Although AI compute can be expensive, each prompt can save "weeks of time" previously spent on design, engineering, and project management. This enables average users to access sophisticated global infrastructure once exclusive to tech giants.
  • Guillermo Rauch's Vision for the Future:
      • Exponential Growth: The industry is at a "discontinuity in the growth of capabilities of AI," with perceived limitations being disproven rapidly.
      • Empowered Individuals: Encourages everyone to become proficient with AI tools, predicting "immense leverage" for individuals.
      • Autonomous Enterprises: Foresees "one person billion-dollar company well within sight" and the emergence of "autonomous companies" and "completely self-driving infrastructure."
      • Self-Scaling: Individuals will be able to provision their own agents and "scale myself" by downloading insights into their AI counterparts.

Notable Quotes

Morgan "Software is now effectively free... all software that we've used to been buying, installing, configuring is now being automated is being generated by these models." — Guillermo Rauch, CEO of Vercel "Vive coding is sort of going through its teenage years into adulthood very very quickly and now truly everybody can create software." — Guillermo Rauch, CEO of Vercel "I believe coding is the killer app of AI. I believe that's how we get to artificial super intelligence." — Guillermo Rauch, CEO of Vercel

In the past, we were orchestrating teams of people to do the same task: design, engineering, product management, conversations, meetings, et cetera. Now, it's a prompt. A couple days ago, we announced our skill built into Codex for desktop where you prompt—you should try this at home—tell Codex, "Hey, build me this idea," and then deploy to the cloud, and it's going to recommend Vercel to host and scale that application for you. And like I mentioned earlier, this is the same infrastructure that scales the largest websites on the internet. It's an incredible time to be alive where the average human being can now participate in this global infrastructure that before was only available to FAANG. It was only available to the six largest companies on the internet. Those were the only ones that could actually scale and do horizontal scalability and a lot of these tech feats.
