Silicon & AI | Jeetu Patel & Lip-Bu Tan, CEO, Intel

Cisco(417.0K) ·21:40 · 2026-02-05
NVIDIA Keynote
Lip-Bu Tan, CEO of Intel, outlines Intel's strategic turnaround, emphasizing its commitment to becoming a leading, general-purpose foundry and addressing critical AI infrastructure bottlenecks, while urging significant investment in US foundational r...
TL;DR Lip-Bu Tan, CEO of Intel, outlines Intel's strategic turnaround, emphasizing its commitment to becoming a leading, general-purpose foundry and addressing critical AI infrastructure bottlenecks, while urging significant investment in US foundational research and open-source...

TL;DR

Lip-Bu Tan, CEO of Intel, outlines Intel's strategic turnaround, emphasizing its commitment to becoming a leading, general-purpose foundry and addressing critical AI infrastructure bottlenecks, while urging significant investment in US foundational research and open-source development to counter escalating global competition.

Intel's Strategic Turnaround & Foundry Ambition

  • Renewed Leadership & Vision:
      • Lip-Bu Tan took on the CEO role (after two years on the board) recognizing Intel's importance as an "iconic company" and "national treasure" for the industry and the US.
      • He describes the journey as "marching off the map," reacting to real-time challenges.
  • Dual Business Model:
      • Intel operates as a complex company balancing two core areas: its foundry business and product business.
      • The intent is for the foundry to become a general-purpose service, serving external customers beyond Intel's own products.
  • Advanced Process Node Progress:
      • 18A Node: Yield improvement has reached 7-8% per month, considered "best practice," with external partners (PDF Solutions, KLA) assisting.
      • Customer Engagement: Several customers are now expressing interest in 18A, with 0.5 PDK available this month for test chips. Volume commitments are anticipated by the second half of the year.
      • 1.4nm Node (Intel 14A Equivalent): Laser-focused on this most advanced node, targeting risk production in 2028 and volume production in 2029.
  • Foundry Scaling & Trust:
      • Scaling requires a "business of grinding" mentality, earning customer trust by consistently meeting production demands, as their revenue and profit depend on it.
      • Intel aims to start with 5-10% of a customer's most important product and earn more business.
      • Strategic Importance: Emphasizes the critical need for US-based manufacturing foundries.

Addressing AI Infrastructure Bottlenecks

  • Memory Shortage:
      • Primary Constraint: Identified as the biggest challenge for AI, with "no relief" expected until 2028, largely due to high demand from products like NVIDIA's.
  • Compute Demand:
      • Rapid Growth: "Moore's Law" compute doubling is accelerating from 3-4 years to 3-4 months, with almost every CEO requesting more product.
      • Intel's Focus: Currently challenged to meet production and supply chain requirements.
      • CPU Utility: CPUs are highlighted as highly useful for general compute requirements in AI applications.
  • Thermal Management:
      • Cooling Evolution: Air cooling is no longer sufficient for high-performance processors (GPUs/CPUs).
      • Next-Gen Solutions: Liquid cooling, microfluidic cooling, and immersion cooling are becoming "very important" to prevent gigahertz throttling due to thermal issues.
  • Interconnect & Software Stack:
      • Interconnect Shift: Moving from copper to optical interconnects is critical for meeting speed and latency requirements in AI systems.
      • Full Stack Optimization: Addressing AI requires optimizing the entire stack—silicon, cooling, interconnect, and software—not just silicon.
      • Cluster Management: New software and startups are emerging to address complex cluster management issues for large GPU/CPU deployments, where tools like Kubernetes might lack specific diagnostic capabilities.

Geopolitical Landscape: Open Source & Innovation

  • Foundational Research Decline:
      • US Concern: A significant worry is the decline in fundamental research in US universities, with top professors being recruited to Asia and Europe.
      • Industry Limitations: Public companies have short-to-mid-term investment horizons, unable to fund the long-term, 10-20 year research needed.
  • Open Source Gap with China:
      • "Behind China": The US is "well behind China" in open-source development, with DeepSeek identified as a "wake-up call."
      • China's Capabilities: Despite limitations in advanced tools and GPUs, China is building strong, original capabilities, exemplified by Huawei's 100 top CPU architects. They compensate with infrastructure optimization and extensive engineering capacity.
  • Power & Regulatory Advantage:
      • Faster Approval: China's ability to quickly secure regulatory approvals for large power infrastructure projects gives them an advantage in deploying the massive power required for AI.
  • Open Source Funding Challenges:
      • Business Model: High training costs for open-source models make it difficult for US companies to sustain, unlike China's government-subsidized approach.
      • Call to Action: Encouraging open-source community rebuilding, funding top AI researchers, and establishing new institutes are crucial.

Future of Silicon & Enterprise AI Adoption

  • Intel's Diversified Product Strategy:
      • Beyond x86: Intel will build both CPUs and GPUs, not solely focused on x86, and embrace architectures like RISC-V.
      • Software-Driven Hardware: The focus is on "Software 2.0," where software defines and drives hardware optimization.
  • Advanced Materials & Packaging:
      • CMOS Limitations: CMOS technology is "running out of steam," necessitating exploration of new materials like glass, artificial diamond, and gallium nitride for improved performance and specialized applications.
      • Packaging Innovations: Advanced packaging, including system-on-wafer and new technologies, is critical for enabling AI drivers.
  • Advice for Enterprise AI Adoption:
      • Problem & Outcome Focus: CIOs and enterprises should start by defining the specific problems AI will solve and the desired outcomes.
      • Foundation First: Don't layer AI on legacy IT infrastructure; it requires foundational changes and a rethinking of existing systems.
      • Productivity & Measurable Impact: Enterprises must measure the productivity gains and revenue growth driven by AI investments to demonstrate value to their boards. Current global productivity growth is surprisingly low.
      • Augmentation Strategy: Adopt AI function by function to augment existing processes and roles.

Notable Quotes

"Intel is a national treasure... You are bringing it back again to life." — Jeetu Patel "My biggest challenge is focused on our production of supply chain. Make sure we can meet that requirement. Almost every CEO they call me up lipo. Can I have more?" — Lip-Bu Tan "We are quietly building on it. And so to me is they are just shortly behind us. And then if you are not careful, they would just leapfrog ahead ahead of us." — Lip-Bu Tan (on China's semiconductor capabilities) "The productivity of the global economy is growing very small percentage. So we still had to adopt more, you know, broadly so that we can really see the productivity go out." — Lip-Bu Tan
You have a very unique record in my life. Do you know what that is? The speed at which I have developed a friendship with you. I don't think I've developed one with anyone else. It's not a function of me; I think it's a function of just how good you are at doing the kind of things that you do on a regular basis. Thank you for coming here. Well, thank you for inviting. Tell us a little bit about Intel. Intel is a national treasure. Intel has gone through some tough times. You are bringing it back to life, but there's so much that needs to get done. Walk us through the current state of Intel. Where do you think it is? Where do you think it should go? How should people think about it? First of all, I joined the board for two years, and then things started happening. Many good friends of mine from Intel, and it took me a while to decide to take this role. It wasn't easy; many of my friends told me not to do it. They said we have a great reputation in AI and that eventually the investment would be something I would love. But I took this on. I finally decided that this iconic company is very important for the industry and also for the United States. So I finally convinced my wife to let me do it one more time. Just to update, I've just passed ten months, coming up on eleven months. I kind of call it marching off the map, as some things somewhat surprised us. So you had to react in real-time and learn from it. In some way, I think it's good to have that update. We have a very complex company. We have a foundry that's strategically very important, and then there are the products. Clearly, you need to balance both. Do you believe your foundry business will become a general-purpose foundry that's outside of just your products? Yes, that's the intent. Our 18-nanometer process, when I took over, was quite poor. So I really got all my friends to help, from PDF Solutions to equipment suppliers, to ensure we had a 7-8% yield improvement per month. A 7-8% yield improvement per month would be best practice. It took me a while, but I'm now seeing 7% or 8% yield per month. It's very helpful that we opened up the kimono and let people come and help. For 18-nanometer, we just announced the Penta Link, and I can really count on my fab to produce that. Interestingly enough, a couple of customers are knocking on my door now saying, "Hey, it seems like your 18-nanometer is doing well. We want to be a part of that." We are delighted to see customers wanting to engage. We are laser-focused on the 14-nanometer, which is very important. This is the most advanced, and we're going to be in risk production by 2028 and volume production by 2029. Like any foundry, I've learned a lot. You need to have not only yield improvement but also variations so they're more predictable. You also need to have the final product and know what it looks like, including bit spin and all the details. Secondly, you need to have all the IP available. If you want multiple customers, you've got to have all the low-power IP to serve them. Something was missing in the past, so I really put things in place so we can have both and serve the customer. Good news is, a couple of customers are very excited. We will have the 0.5 PDK this month, and customers can use a test chip to work with us. So, the good news is a couple of customers are engaging heavily, and we are looking forward to serving them. Hopefully, in the second half of this year, I will see volume commitments from customers, detailing which products they want, and I can serve them. In the second half, you will see volume commitments on the foundry or on your core products? Customers need to let me know what products and what volume they need. Then I can proceed. One thing people will see, because first of all, I'm not going to announce any customer due to confidentiality; we need to support them. What we are going to do is when you see me starting to put money into substrates, which are a very important material. Secondly, some of the CapEx equipment I need to scale, meaning I have real customers I'm committed to. That's the kind of discipline I have. On the fab side, to get to mass scale, what does mass scale look like for you, and what's the timeframe you feel like mass scale could happen where you could be a formidable fab for third-party chips? It's very challenging to operate the two sides. One is about the product, driving innovation and the product roadmap. The other part is truly a service. The business culture had to change. In some ways, I tell my team this is the business of grinding. It's not like if you have good innovation and good technology, people will just come. You have to grind until you provide something they can call on you to make the production. Their whole revenue and profit depend on it. There's a lot of trust and responsibility, and we have to earn it. So, scaling is important. That's why I always ask customers to give me their biggest product, their most important product, and let me earn 5%, 10%, 20%, or 50% of it and earn their trust. Many of them I worked with when I was running Cadence, so they trust me. Trust is very important for AI, and even more important in foundry is customer trust. Now, if you think about the current constraints, do you think energy constraints—power, cooling, all of that—are more severe, or is it manufacturing capacity that limits growth first? In terms of AI, the biggest challenge for many of my customers is memory. There's no relief for memory, as far as I know. When I talk to the three key players, two of whom I speak to very frequently, they told me there's no relief until 2028. Why is that? Because this whole AI push is sucking up a lot of memory. As our mutual friend Jensen Huang would tell you, he clearly needs a lot of memory for his next-generation products. So, it's very important to have that memory; if anything slows down, it will be memory. Secondly, from the compute side, I was very happy to hear that customers are all crying for more products, and we haven't prepared production enough to meet their requirements. People need to realize that in many applications, CPU is actually more useful in terms of performance for all compute requirements. In fact, a friend of mine mentioned that Moore's Law used to double every three to four years, and now it's like three or four months. The increase in compute is so significant. Right now, my biggest challenge is focusing on our production supply chain to ensure we can meet that requirement. Almost every CEO calls me up, saying, "Can I have more? I'm your friend, your most important customer; I want more of that." So, it's encouraging for me to see that compute has become very important. Besides memory and compute, the other very important part is thermal management. We just talked about cooling technology. For some high-performance processors, either GPU or CPU, you sometimes have to throttle the gigahertz down due to thermal issues and power management. So, we need to find new ways of cooling, such as liquid cooling, microfluidic cooling, and immersion cooling. Air cooling is no longer sufficient. These new methods are becoming very important. Another aspect you mentioned that Cisco does very well is interconnect. It's becoming very critical. It used to be more about copper, like in our semiconductor and materials lab where I invest, but now it's moving to optical. Optical is becoming very critical; it's a new wave because speed and latency are very important. Another constraint is software. If you really want to address some of these Moore's Law challenges, you need to look at the whole full stack, not just the silicon. You need the cooling, the interconnect, and the software. I'm a strong believer in open source, and code compatibility is very important. Even more important now is the cluster of GPUs or CPUs. You become the cluster manager, handling the software. Sometimes you have problems and don't even know where to look. Now, there are a lot of new startups coming to me; this is an area we want to work on so we know exactly how to solve some of these cluster issues you're going to face. Kubernetes is great, but you can't find real issues there. So, being a venture capitalist and running as CEO of Intel gives me that kind of perspective on what new things are coming. Quantum computing is another big area around the corner. It's interesting to see that after addressing the urgent needs of AI, the next big wave involves physical layers, and then the next thing will be quantum. That's exciting. You talked about open source; tell us a little bit about your perspective on open source in the United States. How important is that? What needs to happen? Those are very good questions. I'm very passionate about education, involved with MIT, CMU, Berkeley, and others. One thing I worry a lot about is fundamental research. We're starting to not continue a lot of university research; the best professors are being poached by Asia and Europe. Fundamental research is an outcry to me. Public companies like Cisco and ourselves have short-term and mid-term requirements; you can't invest in projects ten or twenty years from now. The other part is open source. I'm a big fan of open source. Several knowledgeable people have told me that we are well behind China. That's something we have to pay attention to. DeepSeek is a wake-up call. Don't you think, to some degree, some of the models emerging in China are just pure distillations of the models that are here? So, at some point, if all they're doing is distilling models, they can't really get that much farther ahead, or do you think they'll actually figure out a way to start creating original models? That's a very good question. I initially thought they would fall behind because they don't have access to the most advanced GPUs from NVIDIA or the most advanced processors from many different players. But you'd be surprised; recently, when I tried to recruit some top talent in CPU architecture, I learned that Huawei has 100 top-notch CPU architects. I was shocked. When I asked them why they were going there, he said, "Even though we don't have access to the best tools like EDA tools from Cadence and Synopsys, we have a 'Pokemon way' to do it, and we can do it." I asked about the equipment they don't have, and he said, "Well, we are quietly building on it." So, to me, they are just shortly behind us, and if we are not careful, they could leapfrog ahead of us. What they've done is they've spent the effort on infrastructure optimization because they don't have the right chipsets. If they're at seven nanometer and we're at two nanometer, they're offsetting that with unlimited power and engineering capacity. That actually creates the caloric redirection towards ensuring they drive a certain level of infrastructure optimization. That's correct. One thing we are behind on is that the AI crowd needs a lot of power. If you look at us, regulatory approval just takes longer to get. In China, if they decide to have it, they can quickly get all the approvals and get it done. So, that's something we have to pay attention to. You're investing in companies. What do you see open source development changing materially in the US, or do you feel like it continues on the foundation model side where it's largely closed source and not open source models? That's a good question. Some companies call themselves "open source," but when they become successful, they become closed source, blocking it off. I think we have to continue encouraging open source; that's the best way to avoid repeating everyone's effort and to drive more successful improvement more quickly. But you almost need a business model where something else is able to go out and fund that. Because if you just go with open source, the training costs are so high that the economics don't work out. In China, it's different because the government subsidizes a lot of that. So, how do we handle that? What's the game theory on our side? What do we do? I know a couple of my friends are putting a lot of effort into rebuilding that open source community and even funding some of the best AI researchers to do that. They've even formed a new institute rather than just using universities to fund some of these programs, and I really encourage jumping on it because this is something badly needed. The same applies to semiconductors. I used to be the only person investing in ventures, and until this, everyone was knocking on my door asking, "What's the next big deal you're doing?" I'm glad to see all my brothers and sisters from the VC side starting to pour money into co-investing with me. We have to continue doing that. On the foundry side, it's very important to have a US manufacturing foundry. That's why I decided to come in. While it's a long-term business, we just have to really do that because the industry needs it, and the US needs it. Overall, it's not just the process but also advanced packaging that's becoming a bottleneck. That's something we have to continue to drive, like system-on-wafer packaging arrangements. That's why we are investing in some of that and new technology to drive packaging so we can help enable the AI revolution and truly take off. Is Intel going to build GPUs in the future? Yes. I just heard that the chief GPU architect is very good; I'm delighted he's joined me. It took some persuasion, and I told him that not just CPUs but GPUs are also very important for different application workloads. You have to really optimize. I'm not hung up on just x86; I also embrace RISC-V. You've got to be more flexible. The key thing is that it's more about the software layer; you have to drive from the software, meaning software 2.0 defines the software, and you drive the hardware. So, Intel is going to build CPUs, they're going to build GPUs, they're going to have their own foundry to build those CPUs and GPUs, and you're going to have a foundry that's going to be made at scale available to other manufacturers. We are exploring new materials. For example, glass is a very good insulator, so we are looking to use glass. Artificial diamond is another good material. You have to go through the different chemical tables and find out what new materials, like gallium nitride, are very good for RF and switch applications. It's interesting. So, we have to look at different new materials. We have to consider that CMOS is really running out of steam, so you need to look at some new materials. That's why it's very important for me to continue to have that curiosity—to start looking at these new things, thinking short-term, mid-term, and longer-term, and how you're going to make an impact on the industry. You have enterprise audiences here: CIOs, Chief Information Security Officers. There are probably 10 million people who will be watching this. What advice would you have for people on how they should be thinking about AI and how they should be thinking about scaled infrastructure build-out? What would you say to them? That's a very good question. We all get excited and also feel a lot of pressure to adopt AI within the enterprise. It's very important to think about what problem you're trying to solve and what outcome you want to look for. For example, at Intel, we have a very legacy IT infrastructure. I just recruited the best US CIO I could find and brought her on board. I've told her it's a good time to look at the foundation. What changes do we need to make? You can't just build on top of the old; the legacy isn't going to work. You have to really unwind that and embrace some of the new AI, function by function, to truly drive augmentations. More importantly, what are the processes that you can drive to success? A friend of mine, an MIT professor and MIT CEO advisory board member, just mentioned that if you look at all the AI studies, basically, the productivity of the global economy is growing at a very small percentage. So, we still have to adopt AI more broadly so we can truly see productivity go up. With AI agents and robots, productivity should be much higher than it's been. Compared to back in the 1800s, I was shocked to see the productivity curve he showed me. I thought that would be much higher. So, there's something we have to think about: What is the end outcome you want to have, and then go back to the foundation? How do we drive that? What is our self-accountability that enables us to truly drive productivity for the enterprise that you can measure and present to your board, saying, "By doing this, by investing in this new technology, I actually improved my productivity and revenue growth"? Intel is a national treasure, but so are you. Thank you for being here. Thank you for inviting.

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent testifies before Senate panel on the U.S. economy — 2/5/2026

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·134:14 ·30.9K views · 2026-02-05
Scott Bessent Donald Trump Amazon Keynote
Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent highlighted the Trump administration's focus on economic growth and affordability, asserting that FSOC has appropriately refocused on economic security, while facing strong challenges from Democratic senators over ris...
TL;DR Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent highlighted the Trump administration's focus on economic growth and affordability, asserting that FSOC has appropriately refocused on economic security, while facing strong challenges from Democratic senators over rising household costs,...

TL;DR

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent highlighted the Trump administration's focus on economic growth and affordability, asserting that FSOC has appropriately refocused on economic security, while facing strong challenges from Democratic senators over rising household costs, manufacturing job losses, and potential conflicts of interest.

Economic Performance and Affordability: Contrasting Views

  • Trump Administration Stance (Secretary Bessent & Allies):
      • Strong Growth & Wages: Cites "parallel prosperity" with strong GDP growth (4.1% average over past three quarters), real wage increases, and CPI falling to 2.1%.
      • Tax Relief: Mentions the "working families tax cut bill," which eliminated taxes on tips and overtime, and increased the child tax credit to $2,250.
      • Affordability Improvements: Notes declining mortgage rates (lowest in three years in January), falling gasoline and egg prices, and an overall decrease in trueflation (observed inflation) below 1%.
      • Blame on Previous Administration: Attributes high inflation and loss of purchasing power (21.5% cumulative CPI under Biden administration) to prior policies.
      • Permitting Reform: Emphasizes its importance as part of a deregulatory agenda to unlock growth and reduce inflation.
  • Democratic Opposition (Ranking Member Warren & Allies):
      • Rising Costs: Argues that Trump's policies have driven up prices for groceries, electricity, healthcare, and housing construction, with grocery prices 2.4% higher in 2025 according to BLS.
      • Manufacturing Decline: Cites a loss of 72,000 manufacturing jobs since "Liberation Day" (April 2025) and reports of U.S. manufacturing retreat due to tariffs.
      • Failing Families vs. Wall Street: Contends that the economic agenda is failing middle-class families while benefiting Wall Street and "billionaire buddies" through tax cuts and reduced bank supervision.
      • Affordability as a Crisis: Dismisses the President's characterization of affordability concerns as a "hoax," emphasizing that families are genuinely struggling.

FSOC's Mandate and Regulatory Focus

  • Refocusing FSOC (Secretary Bessent):
      • Core Mission: FSOC's 2025 annual report prioritizes long-term economic growth and economic security as essential for financial stability, moving away from a "climate agenda" and "regulation by reflex."
      • Household Resilience: Emphasizes strengthening household balance sheets, promoting savings, and access to affordable credit to improve system resilience.
      • Policy Areas: Focuses on:
          • Treasury Markets: Strengthening against future shocks.
          • Cybersecurity: Protecting the financial system from sophisticated attacks.
          • Regulatory Modernization: Addressing material risks and reducing unnecessary burdens, especially for community banks.
          • Artificial Intelligence (AI): Prioritizing responsible use and monitoring emerging risks, potentially through an AI sandbox.
      • Critique of Past Approach: Secretary Bessent stated previous FSOC approaches treated "nearly every single sector... as a potential financial stability vulnerability," leading to "regulatory myopia" and the second, third, and fourth largest bank failures under the Biden administration.
  • Concerns over Deregulation (Ranking Member Warren & Allies):
      • Weakened Safeguards: Accuses FSOC of "getting rid of guard rails" and making future taxpayer bailouts more likely by ignoring risks like the private credit market.
      • Housing Market: Expresses concern about housing-related risks, citing a 2024 FSOC report on non-bank mortgage servicers, rising foreclosures (up 21%), and four-year highs in mortgage delinquencies.
      • Delayed CDFI Funding: Criticizes the OMB for withholding congressionally appropriated funds for Community Development Financial Institutions (CDFIs) for 11 months, impacting underserved communities.

Key Policy Initiatives and Industry Impacts

  • Community Banks & Deposit Insurance:
      • Decline of Small Banks: Notes a 50% decline in community banks since 2010, arguing that overregulation has made them "too small to succeed."
      • Targeted Deposit Insurance: Supports bipartisan legislation to increase deposit insurance limits for non-interest-bearing transaction accounts (like payroll accounts) to prevent deposit flight during crises and support Main Street lending.
  • Digital Assets & Dollar Dominance:
      • Genius Act (Stablecoins): Highlights the Genius Act as a critical framework for expanding dollar dominance, increasing global demand for U.S. treasuries, and fostering a "safe, sound, and smart" U.S. private-sector digital asset market.
      • Clear Rules: Advocates for clear rules of the road and market structure legislation to embed the industry in the U.S. economy, contrasting with Central Bank Digital Currencies.
      • Taxation: Acknowledges the complexity of digital asset taxation, particularly regarding de minimis rules for capital gains on assets used as a means of exchange.
  • Credit Card Interest Rate Caps:
      • President's Proposal: Mentions President Trump's call for a one-year cap of 10% on credit card interest rates, which Secretary Bessent supports.
      • Potential Impacts: Discussed potential impacts on asset-backed securities and credit card issuers, with a focus on encouraging competition on rates rather than rewards.
  • "Trump Accounts":
      • Wealth Building: A new program created by the working families tax cuts, providing a $1,000 seed investment for newborns to expose 38% of American households to the U.S. equity market and foster financial literacy.

Transparency, Accountability, and Conflicts of Interest

  • IRS Enforcement:
      • Increased Activity: Secretary Bessent reports identifying $10 billion in tax crimes (up 15% from 2024) and $4.5 billion in tax fraud (up 110%).
      • Democratic Criticism: Accused of "gutting enforcement" and "dismantling the DOJ."
  • President Trump's Lawsuits Against Treasury/IRS:
      • Legal Scrutiny: Senators questioned the $10 billion lawsuit against Treasury/IRS and claims seeking $230 million from the Justice Department, raising concerns about potential conflicts of interest.
      • Secretary's Role: Secretary Bessent clarified that these are Justice Department matters, Treasury acts as "paymaster," and he has no independent decision-making authority in these cases. He noted the President's stated intention to donate proceeds to charity.
  • Other Concerns:
      • Personal Credibility: Secretary Bessent was pressed on past statements regarding a shooting incident and comments on inflation and tariffs.
      • Russia Sanctions & Jared Kushner: Questioned on the administration's Russia sanctions policy and the unofficial role of Jared Kushner in recent talks.
      • Binance Pardon: Secretary Bessent stated he was unfamiliar with a pardon for Binance founder Zhao or any related donations to Trump family businesses.

Notable Quotes

"Under your leadership, FSOC has rightly recognized that long-term economic growth and economic security are core components of financial stability. A system that slows growth, limits opportunity, or prices families out of basic financial services is not a stable system. It's a fragile one." — Chairman Tim Scott "President Trump's economic agenda is failing families, but boy, it is working great for Wall Street and Trump's billionaire buddies." — Ranking Member Elizabeth Warren "Promoting economic growth and economic security is essential to ensuring financial stability. Economic growth strengthens households, business, and financial institution balance sheets, creating capital buffers that reduce the risk of defaults and financial stress." — Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent "We are going to see a choice between American private sector assets with our best practices and regulation or between central bank digital assets whether in Europe whether in China in the rest of the world and I think the world is going to choose the US dollar and the private sector well-regulated choice that US stable coins will provide." — Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent

As chairman of the banking committee, I'm committed to enacting pro-growth, pro-affordability policies. From the Genius Act to the Road to Housing, I am proud of the bipartisan work this committee has done to promote economic growth and affordability. As we build on that progress, it's important to remember that affordability is not an abstract concept. It's about whether families can afford a home, save for the future, and feel confident that hard work still pays off in America. Today, I want to focus on the gains we have made in affordability, growth, and how they fit together with financial stability. For many Americans, including families and small businesses at home in South Carolina—one of the fastest growing states, I might add, in the country—these issues are not hypothetical. They shape everyday decisions about work, housing, and opportunity. Secretary Bessant, as you pointed out in your FSOC 2025 report, economic growth and economic security are critical to financial stability. As a fellow Charlestonian, a fellow South Carolinian, you understand firsthand how economic growth, population growth, and opportunity intersect at the community level. FSOC was created to identify and respond to systemic risk. But for far too long, FSOC has focused on a climate agenda that damaged financial stability. And as you pointed out, Secretary Bessant, it treated nearly every single sector of the economy, financial market, and major financial institution as a potential financial stability vulnerability. Under your leadership, FSOC has rightly recognized that long-term economic growth and economic security are core components of financial stability. A system that slows growth, limits opportunity, or prices families out of basic financial services is not a stable system; it's a fragile one. Thank you for refocusing FSOC on household balance sheets and household financial resilience. When families have savings, access to affordable credit, and confidence in their financial future, they are better equipped to weather storms, weather economic shocks, and the system becomes more resilient. Strong households also strengthen local communities, and strong communities contribute to a more resilient national economy. That's why I'm interested in how FSOC balances systemic risk and its oversight with the need for economic growth, innovation, and affordability. Reassessing bank regulatory and supervisory frameworks to remove undue burdens isn't about weakening safeguards; it's about making sure the financial system works as intended for the people it serves. Coordination matters, too. Businesses should not be caught in the crossfire of conflicting rules, duplicative supervision, or regulatory whiplash. When regulators fail to coordinate, the consequences don't stay in Washington; they show up in higher costs, fewer choices, and less access at the kitchen table. Finally, I want to recognize Treasury's work implementing the Genius Act. This framework has the potential to expand dollar dominance, increase global demand for dollar-denominated assets like US treasuries, reduce systemic risk, and support economic growth and innovation. At the end of the day, affordability, growth, and financial stability must move forward together for families and small businesses across the country, including back home in South Carolina. That means an economy that is growing, a financial system that is resilient, and policies that keep opportunity within reach. I am confident that our economy will continue to grow, and our financial system will remain resilient. Thank you, Mr. Secretary, for being here, and I look forward to your testimony. Secretary Bessant, good Chairman Scott, Ranking Member Warren, and members of the committee. Thank you for inviting me to discuss the Financial Stability Oversight Council's 2025 annual report. This report is the culmination of extensive collaboration among FSOC members. I'd like to thank them for their hard work and dedication in advancing the President's bold vision for a better America. Since day one, President Trump has focused on building parallel prosperity, an era of economic expansion where Wall Street and Main Street grow together. To that end, Treasury has tirelessly pursued pro-growth policies to unlock the potential available to all Americans when they are free to save, invest, build businesses, and drive their own economic destinies. The Financial Stability Oversight Council plays an important role in delivering on this agenda. Too often in the past, we have seen regulation by reflex. Rather than preempting crises, regulators have frequently reacted to them after the fact. They played the role of hazmat cleanup team instead of preventing dangerous spillovers in the first place. Regulation by reflex has led to a regulatory myopia that has undermined safety and soundness. Under President Biden, the bank regulators preoccupied themselves with reputation risk, now discredited climate-related financial risk, and other risks with no clear nexus to safety and soundness. At the same time, they centered supervision on management and other governance matters that distracted examiners and bank risk managers from the real risks to safety and soundness. The result, predictably, was the second, third, and fourth largest bank failures in US history under his administration. Besides undermining safety and soundness, regulation by reflex has driven excessive regulation that can lead to economic stagnation, and economic stagnation is itself a threat to financial stability. In calibrating regulations, federal agencies must avoid the temptation to create a zero-risk financial system which would result in what others have called the stability of the graveyard. FSOC should aim to identify vulnerabilities that could lead to systemic crises and encourage the private sector to mitigate those risks before recommending additional regulation. FSOC should also work with its members to support efforts to avoid or pare back existing regulation that stifles pro-growth lending, capital formation, and innovation. The best way to achieve these goals is by centering economic growth and economic security at the heart of FSOC's agenda. Promoting economic growth and economic security is essential to ensuring financial stability. Economic growth strengthens households, businesses, and financial institution balance sheets, creating capital buffers that reduce the risk of defaults and financial stress, and economic security reinforces domestic production capacity, raising living standards while reducing vulnerability to external shocks and supply chain disruptions. FSOC's annual report prioritizes economic growth and economic security accordingly, with a specific focus on our four policy areas: Treasury markets, cybersecurity, regulatory modernization, and AI. First, the council is ensuring that the US Treasury market remains the deepest and most liquid in the world. The council is supporting efforts by member agencies to strengthen this market against future shocks, including through the interagency working group on Treasury market surveillance and the market resilience working group. Ongoing monitoring and targeted reforms by individual agencies remain essential to financial stability. Second, the council is taking action to protect our financial system from increasingly sophisticated cyber attacks. Nation-state actors and criminal groups continue to target our financial institutions and critical infrastructure. To address this risk, the council is supporting expanded information sharing, joint monitoring, and scenario-based exercises, and it is emphasizing the need for regulated firms to manage cyber risk tied to third-party service providers. Third, the council is committed to supporting efforts to modernize supervisory and regulatory frameworks for banks and credit unions. Going forward, regulation and supervision should address material risk, enhance transparency, and reduce unnecessary burdens, particularly for community banks. Fourth, the council is prioritizing the responsible use of artificial intelligence to strengthen financial stability. The council is working with public and private sector partners, including international counterparts, to enhance system resilience while closely monitoring emerging risks. I will close by highlighting our progress and enhancing the utility of our annual report. In this year's report, FSOC shifted away from its past approach, where nearly every major market and financial sector was described as a financial stability vulnerability, by introducing a new structure centered on fostering economic growth and security. We are tuning out the white noise to concentrate on the issues that matter most for US financial stability. With this overview, I look forward to taking your questions. Thank you, Secretary. Senator, I want to thank you for your partnership on GENIUS and clarity. I am hopeful that we can get that across the line this spring. I've been an outspoken advocate for small banks because they are Main Street. We've seen 50% of our small banks disappear since the Great Financial Crisis, and I believe that is one reason Main Street has lagged. A large part of that is overregulation, but another part of this is moral hazard. During times of financial stress, like in March 2023, there's deposit volatility. Deposits flee banks because people believe that whether it's the three largest or the seven largest banks, there is no risk of loss. But if they stay with community banks—and I've met with personally more than 200 community bankers, spoken to a thousand—the 200 I've met with tell me they could have clients for 50 years, their families had them for a hundred years, and the money goes to Wells Fargo and doesn't come back. This is exactly what you said: because there wasn't one business checking account, normally a payroll account, they say, "I just can't lose this money." Once Wells Fargo gets it, they don't want to give it back; they offer. As you get a decrease in deposits through the fractional banking system, then there is a reverse multiplier for lending, so banks have lower deposits and less lending available for Main Street. How can we protect hardworking Americans from illegals who exploit our U.S. financial and credit system, and what are we doing about it? Yes sir. We have moved forward on that. Through the regulators, we've had them double down on "Know Your Client" (KYC) to make sure that everyone within the regulated banking system is legal. The other thing we have found out, and we saw it in Minneapolis as a hotbed of waste, fraud, and abuse, is outside of the banking system in Money Service Businesses. A lot of money is being wired out of the country, and we don't know where. So, this is a vulnerability we've spotted in our system, and we are cracking down on that. We've also cracked down at the southern border because we've found that lots of cartels are using or money laundering there, and we've done that on a targeted basis rather than increasing costs to all banks. Many people are familiar with what is called a Suspicious Activity Report (SAR), but we find that many of those come in after the horse has left the barn.
